Such as the theory of ownership and its types and causes, the theory of contractual connection (i.e. contracts) and the general foundations upon which they are based; the theory of contractual conditions of suspension, restriction, and addition; the theory of legal supports and what branches from them in the civil field of invalidity, corruption, and suspension; the theory of legal capacity; the theory of guarantee; the theory of disposal and its authority; and similar fundamental principles and major objective theories that are pillars of fiqh and supports of its rulings. Their parts are scattered throughout the chapters of fiqh; in each chapter what pertains to it is mentioned, but their parts can be plucked and gathered from their places and a series of theories can be formulated that preface the science of fiqh, thus being keys to the locks of its issues and providing the student with a quick ability in understanding that he used to need a long journey through all the chapters of fiqh to acquire. Therefore I saw it necessary to present before this fiqh series a general fiqh introduction (Madkhal Fiqhi 'Amm) comprising three sections: 1 - An introductory and historical overview of Islamic fiqh, its sources, the emergence of ijtihad schools within it, the reasons for their differences, the value of those differences in legislative wealth, the movement of codification in it, the strata of its authors up to the emergence of the Majalla, and about the breadth of this Islamic fiqh with all its ijtihadat, its development and its suitability to meet the needs of the times if one wishes to benefit from it, and if concerned researchers bestow upon it new service and modern cultivation that yields the hidden wealth in its mines and precious minerals. 2 - The basic theories in the foundations of fiqh rulings. They are the great foundational systems and pillars that are considered the supports and pillars of fiqh, and we have already pointed out the necessity of gathering them and prefacing fiqh with them.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface (خطبة الكتاب) to al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. The author opens by stating that his work takes the Hanafi madhhab as the primary basis because it is the official school applied in the judiciary, though he also references other madhhabs where needed. His main objective is to re-formulate Islamic fiqh by constructing general theories from its rules and principles, analogous to the general theory of obligations in modern Western legal systems. He argues that the traditional branch-based (فروعي) style of fiqh, which lists individual rulings without linking them to overarching principles, has become indigestible for contemporary students. The author identifies the root cause of students' difficulty: fiqh's branch issues are connected to universal principles (أصول كلية) and general theories, but these are dispersed across various chapters, so a beginner cannot see the big picture. He lists examples of such theories: ownership, contracts, contractual conditions (suspension, restriction, addition), legal validity/invalidity, legal capacity, guarantee, and the authority of disposal. These are the pillars of fiqh. By gathering them into a coherent series, he aims to provide a key to understanding fiqh and accelerate the student's grasp. Therefore, he introduces his 'General Fiqh Introduction' (مدخل فقهي عام) divided into three sections, though only two are fully listed here: (1) an introductory and historical overview covering sources, madhhabs, codification, and the Majalla; (2) the basic theories underlying fiqh rulings. The third section presumably follows on the next page. This preface sets the stage for a modern, systematic, and pedagogical presentation of Hanafi fiqh, focusing on general theories rather than isolated cases.

Key Terms
نظرية عامة (nazariyya 'amma): A comprehensive theoretical framework built from fiqh rules and principles, analogous to a 'general theory' in modern legal systems (e.g., the general theory of obligations).
أسلوب فروعي (uslub furu'i): The traditional method of presenting fiqh by enumerating individual branch rulings (furu') without explicitly linking them to overarching principles or theories.
مدخل فقهي عام (madkhal fiqhi 'amm): A general introduction to fiqh that presents the foundational theories and principles before delving into specific chapters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core purpose of al-Zarqa's second section (basic theories) in his Madkhal?
A) To list all rulings of fiqh in summary
B) To present the general theories that underpin fiqh rulings, gathered from various chapters
C) To critique the views of other madhhabs
D) To provide a translation of the Majalla

Key Takeaway
Al-Zarqa's Madkhal rebuilds fiqh education by gathering scattered theories into a systematic preface, providing students with a conceptual map before delving into branches.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Need for a General Jurisprudential Introduction (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm)
Author's preface diagnosing the need for a general jurisprudential introduction and outlining its structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three components of the author's proposed general jurisprudential introduction and explain why a systematic introduction is necessary for studying fiqh.

Core Idea
Fiqh's scattered principles need a unified introduction; the madkhal gathers them into history, theories, and maxims.

The author opens by identifying a key obstacle for students: fiqh's rulings are tied to underlying theories that are scattered across chapters. Without a bird's-eye view, understanding remains fragmented. He therefore proposes a three-part introduction (المدخل الفقهي العام) to equip students with the necessary foundations before delving into detailed rulings. This lesson examines his diagnosis and the structure of his proposed solution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pedagogical problem
Read the opening lines to understand why students find fiqh difficult. The author argues that subsidiary rulings connect to universal principles and major theories that are scattered across chapters.
The key phrase: 'مسائله الفرعية تتصل بأصول كلية ونظريات عامة أساسية'.
2
List the key theories mentioned
Scan for theories like ownership, contracts, conditions, sanctions, capacity, liability, and dispositive authority. These are the building blocks that a student must know.
These theories are examples; the author says 'ونحو ذلك' indicating there are more.
3
State the author's solution
The author proposes a 'general jurisprudential introduction' (مدخل فقهي عام) that precedes the detailed study of fiqh. Identify the three sections he outlines.
Look for 'يشتمل على ثلاثة أقسام'.
4
Describe each section
Section 1: Historical and definitional introduction about Islamic fiqh, its sources, schools, and codification. Section 2: The basic theories (النظريات الأساسية) that underpin fiqh rulings. Section 3: The universal maxims (القواعد الكلية) from the Majalla, rearranged with commentary.
The author emphasizes that these are the 'keys to the locks of its issues'.
5
Note the series structure
The work is part of a series titled 'Islamic Fiqh in Its New Garment'. The first two parts are the Madkhal; the third is an introduction to the general theory of civil rulings; the fourth was planned but not published in full.
The author later published separate studies on the theory of abuse of right and harmful act.

وقد كان من أعظم الأسباب في استغلاق الفقه على الطلاب خلال دراستهم الجامعية أن مسائله الفرعية تتصل بأصول كلية ونظريات عامة أساسية، يشترك في العلاقة بكل منها كثير من أبواب الفقه، ويتوقف على الإحاطة بها فهم مسائله وأحكامه، والطالب في بَدء دراسته يجهلها جميعاً. وذلك كنظرية الملكية وأنواعها وأسبابها ونظرية الارتباط التعاقدي، أي العقود، وما ترتكز عليه من أسس عامة ؛ ونظرية الشروط العقدية من تعليق وتقييد وإضافة ؛ ونظرية المؤيدات التشريعية وما يتفرع عنها في الحقل المدني من قضية البطلان والفساد والتوقف ؛ ونظرية الأهلية ؛ ونظرية الضمان ؛ ونظرية العزف وسلطانه، ونحو ذلك من المبادىء الأساسية والنظريات الموضوعية الكبرى التي هي من أركان الفقه ودعائم أحكامه، وهي منثورة الأجزاء في غضون الأبواب الفقهية يذكر منها في كل باب ما يتعلق به، ولكنها يمكن أن تقتطف أجزاؤها وتجمع من أماكنها وتصاغ منها سلسلة نظريات يصدر بها علم الفقه فتكون مفاتيح لأغلاق مسائله وتزود الطالب فيه بملكة في الفهم عاجلة كان يحتاج في اكتسابها إلى سير طويل في جميع فصول الفقه لذلك رأيت من الضروري أن أقدم بين يدي هذه السلسلة الفقهية مدخلاً فقهياً عاماً يشتمل على ثلاثة أقسام :
١ - مقدمة تعريفية وتاريخية عن الفقه الإسلامي، ومصادره، ونشأة المذاهب الاجتهادية فيه وأسباب اختلافاتها وقيمة تلك الاختلافات في الثروة التشريعية وحركة التدوين فيه وطبقات مؤلفاته إلى بروز المجلة وعن سَعَة هذا الفقه الإسلامي بمجموع اجتهاداته وعن تطوره وصلوحه لوفاء حاجات العصور إذا أريدت الاستفادة منه، وجاد عليه الباحثون المعنيون بخدمة جديدة وحراثة حديثة يجود على أثرها ما في مناجمه من ثروة دفينة، ومعادن ثمينة.
٢ - النظريات الأساسية في مباني الأحكام الفقهية.
وهي النظم والركائز التأسيسية الكبرى التي تعتبر دعائم الفقه وأركانه وأشرنا آنفاً إلى ضرورة جمعها وتصدير الفقه بها.
٣ - القواعد الكلية التي صدرت بها المجلة مرتبة ترتيباً جديداً وموضحة بشرح موجز .
وقد جعلت لهذه السلسلة اسماً عاماً متسلسلاً تشترك فيه جميع أجزائها وهو:
الفقه الإسلامي في ثوبه الجديد
ثم جعلت لكل جزء عنواناً خاصاً به يدل على موضوعه ومباحثه : فالجزء آن الأول والثاني :هما: المدخل الفقهي العام.
والجزء الثالث هو المدخل إلى النظرية العامة للأحكام المدنية في الفقه الإسلامي وهو بمثابة باب تمهيدي لصياغة تلك النظرية العامة الجامعة، ويشتمل على ثلاثة فصول تلقي نظرة عامة على فكرة الحق والالتزام والأموال والأشخاص في الفقه الإسلامي.
والجزء الرابع هو النظرية العامة للأحكام المدنية في الفقه الإسلامي على غرار النظرية العامة للالتزامات في الفقه الأجنبي
(۱) الجزء الرابع هذا (الذي موضوعه عرض النظرية العامة للحقوق والالتزامات في الفقه الإسلامي)، لم يصدر بسبب صدور القانون المدني السوري الذي حل محل مجلة الأحكام العدلية وانصرافنا إلى تدريس النظرية العامة في القانون المدني المذكور.
لكني فيما بعد نشرت بعض مضامين النظرية العامة منفصلة، فمن ذلك : ١ - نظرية التعسف في استعمال الحق في الشريعة الإسلامية (صياغة قانونية لها لمشروع قانون مدني مستمد من الفقه الإسلامي. ٢ - الفعل الضار من مصادر (الالتزام) مؤصلاً ومستمداً من الفقه الإسلامي بمذاهبه المختلفة .
وقد كانت أُلفت لجنة خبراء بجامعة الدول العربية بالدائرة القانونية لوضوع مشروع قانون مدني موحد للبلاد العربية على أساس الفقه الإسلامي، في أواخر السبعينيات وأوائل الثمانينيات وكنت أحد أعضائها، فوضعنا مشروعاً للنظرية العامة كاملة، نصوصاً وتأصيلاً، ومما قدمته أنا فيها :
١ - الباب التمهيدي عن الحق والالتزام والأشخاص، وفيه نظرية التعسف المشار إليها .
One of the greatest reasons for the difficulty of fiqh for students during their university studies is that its subsidiary issues are connected to universal principles and basic general theories, many chapters of fiqh share a relationship with each of them, and understanding its issues and rulings depends on comprehensive knowledge of them, while the student at the beginning of his study is ignorant of all of them. Such as the theory of ownership, its types and causes; the theory of contractual connection, i.e., contracts, and the general foundations upon which they are based; the theory of contractual conditions, including suspension, restriction, and deferment; the theory of legislative sanctions and what branches from them in the civil field regarding nullity, corruption, and suspension; the theory of legal capacity; the theory of liability; the theory of dispositive authority and its power, and similar basic principles and major substantive theories that are pillars of fiqh and supports of its rulings. They are scattered in parts within the fiqh chapters; in each chapter what relates to it is mentioned, but their parts can be plucked and gathered from their places and a series of theories can be formulated from them with which the science of fiqh can be prefaced, serving as keys to the locks of its issues and providing the student with an immediate understanding that he would otherwise need a long journey through all sections of fiqh to acquire. Therefore, I saw it necessary to present before this jurisprudential series a general jurisprudential introduction comprising three sections:
1 - A definitional and historical introduction about Islamic fiqh, its sources, the emergence of the ijtihad schools in it, the reasons for their differences, the value of those differences in the legislative wealth, the movement of codification in it, the layers of its works until the emergence of the Majalla, and about the breadth of this Islamic fiqh with all its ijtihadat, and about its development and suitability to fulfill the needs of the times if benefit is sought from it, and if concerned researchers came upon it with new service and modern cultivation that would then reveal what is in its mines of hidden wealth and precious metals.
2 - The basic theories concerning the foundations of fiqh rulings.
They are the major foundational systems and pillars that are considered the supports and pillars of fiqh, and we have earlier pointed to the necessity of collecting them and prefacing fiqh with them.
3 - The universal maxims with which the Majalla was prefaced, arranged in a new order and explained with a brief commentary.
And I have given this series a general sequential name shared by all its parts, which is:
Islamic Fiqh in Its New Garment
Then I gave each part a specific title indicating its subject and topics: The first and second parts: The General Jurisprudential Introduction.
The third part is the Introduction to the General Theory of Civil Rulings in Islamic Fiqh, which serves as a preliminary door for formulating that comprehensive general theory, and it includes three chapters that give a general overview of the concept of right, obligation, property, and persons in Islamic fiqh.
The fourth part is the General Theory of Civil Rulings in Islamic Fiqh, modeled after the general theory of obligations in foreign fiqh.
(1) This fourth part (whose subject is presenting the general theory of rights and obligations in Islamic fiqh) was not published due to the issuance of the Syrian Civil Code which replaced the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya and our diversion to teaching the general theory in the aforementioned civil code.
However, I later published some contents of the general theory separately, including: 1 - The theory of abuse of right in Islamic Sharia (a legal formulation for a draft civil code derived from Islamic fiqh). 2 - Harmful act as a source of (obligation), rooted and derived from Islamic fiqh with its various schools.
And a committee of experts from the Arab League in the legal department was formed to develop a unified civil code for Arab countries based on Islamic fiqh, in the late seventies and early eighties, and I was one of its members. So we drafted a complete project for the general theory, texts and roots, and among what I contributed in it:
1 - The preliminary chapter on right, obligation, and persons, including the aforementioned theory of abuse.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to his multi-volume work. He begins by diagnosing a pedagogical problem: students find fiqh difficult because its numerous subsidiary rulings are connected to overarching principles and theories that are scattered across different chapters. Without first understanding these foundational theories, students cannot properly grasp the rulings. He lists several key theories: ownership, contracts, conditions, sanctions, capacity, liability, and dispositive authority. These are the building blocks of fiqh, but they are not taught systematically. To remedy this, he proposes a 'general jurisprudential introduction' (مدخل فقهي عام) consisting of three sections. The first section is a historical and definitional overview of Islamic fiqh, its sources, schools, codification, and the Majalla. The second section gathers the basic theories that underpin fiqh rulings. The third section presents the universal maxims (القواعد الكلية) from the Majalla, rearranged and briefly explained. The author then outlines his series 'Islamic Fiqh in Its New Garment' (الفقه الإسلامي في ثوبه الجديد), specifying the contents of each part. He notes that the fourth part, on the general theory of civil rulings, was not published due to the Syrian Civil Code replacing the Majalla, but he later published separate studies. He also mentions his involvement in an Arab League committee for a unified civil code based on Islamic fiqh. This passage is essential background for understanding the author's methodology and the structure of the subsequent lessons.

Key Terms
المدخل الفقهي العام (al-madkhal al-fiqhi al-‘amm): A general jurisprudential introduction that provides the foundational principles, theories, and maxims necessary for understanding Islamic fiqh, presented before the detailed study of legal rulings.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa‘id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that govern many individual rulings; here specifically referring to the 99 maxims of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya.
النظريات الأساسية (al-nazariyyat al-asasiyya): The basic theories that form the foundation of fiqh rulings, such as ownership, contracts, capacity, liability, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fiqh history, basic theories, and universal maxims
B) Sources of fiqh, ijtihad schools, and the Majalla
C) Ownership, contracts, and capacity
D) The general theory of obligations, the theory of abuse of right, and harmful act

Key Takeaway
The madkhal is essential for systematic fiqh study; it compiles history, core theories, and universal maxims to unlock a student's understanding.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Understanding al-Zarqa's Methodological Reform in Presenting Fiqh
Author outlines the series structure and methodological approach

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key methodological features of Mustafa al-Zarqa's series and explain its significance for modern fiqh presentation.

Core Idea
Shar’a reformulates classical fiqh into a subject-based system while preserving original sources—a paradigm shift for muftis.

This lesson examines the opening of Mustafa al-Zarqa's multi-volume work 'Islamic Jurisprudence in Its New Garment'. The author presents a groundbreaking approach: moving from the traditional 'branch' (furū') arrangement to a modern, subject-based (mawḍū'ī) organization, while ensuring every ruling is traceable to its classical source. This methodology is crucial for contemporary muftis who must navigate between classical authority and modern legal frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the series structure
Read the first few lines to determine how many parts are planned and what each part covers. Note the intended sequence: introduction, general theory of civil rulings, and named contracts.
The author explicitly mentions four parts, though the fourth was not published.
2
Analyze the methodological shift
Compare the author's 'branch form' (al-shakl al-furū'ī) to his new 'subject form' (al-shakl al-mawḍū'ī). Recognize this as a structural reordering of fiqh topics.
This is a key innovation: presenting fiqh thematically rather than by school-based chapters.
3
Examine source attribution policy
Find where the author commits to citing original sources. Understand the balance between modern presentation and classical fidelity.
He states he will attribute rulings except for well-known ones, ensuring students can consult classical texts.
4
Identify the collaborative and contextual elements
Note the mention of the League of Arab States committee and the Syrian Civil Code. This shows the author's engagement with modern legal codification.
The work was partly inspired by a need for a unified Arab civil code based on fiqh.

يروي الروية في التخطيط والإحاطة والترتيب والتهذيب والتنقيح.

- الفعل الضار من مصادر الالتزام الآنف الذكر . وقد نشرت في آخره نصوص النظرية العامة كاملة الباب التمهيدي ومصادر الالتزام ثم الأحكام حتى انقضاء الالتزام وأسباب انقضائه).
ثم توقفت الدائرة القانونية عن متابعة مشروع القانون المدني الموحد مؤصلاً تأصيلاً كاملاً على الفقه الإسلامي بالصورة المثالية التي وضعنا بها النظرية العامة. ولذلك أنا عندئذ نصوص النظرية العامة في آخر كتابي المذكور (الفعل الضار) كيلا تضيع ثمرات هذا العمل المجيد .
نشرت
Sermon of the Book
3 - The universal rules with which the Journal began, arranged in a new order and clarified with a brief explanation.
I have given this series a general, sequential name shared by all its parts, which is:
Islamic Jurisprudence in Its New Garment
Then I gave each part a specific title indicating its subject and topics: The first and second parts are: The General Introduction to Fiqh.
The third part is the Introduction to the General Theory of Civil Rulings in Islamic Jurisprudence, which serves as a preliminary chapter for formulating that comprehensive general theory, and it includes three chapters that give an overview of the concept of right, obligation, property, and persons in Islamic jurisprudence.
The fourth part is the General Theory of Civil Rulings in Islamic Jurisprudence along the lines of the general theory of obligations in foreign jurisprudence
(1)
(1) This fourth part (whose subject is the presentation of the general theory of rights and obligations in Islamic jurisprudence) was not published due to the issuance of the Syrian Civil Code which replaced the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah and our turning to teaching the general theory in the mentioned civil code.
But later I published some contents of the general theory separately, including: 1 - The theory of abuse of right in Islamic law (a legal formulation for a draft civil law derived from Islamic jurisprudence). 2 - The harmful act from the sources of (obligation) rooted and derived from Islamic jurisprudence with its different schools.
A committee of experts was formed by the League of Arab States in the Legal Department to draft a unified civil code for Arab countries based on Islamic jurisprudence in the late 1970s and early 1980s, and I was one of its members. We produced a complete draft of the general theory, texts and rooting, and among what I contributed to it:
1 - The preliminary chapter on right, obligation, and persons, including the theory of abuse referred to.
With the completion of these four parts, God willing - which include general investigations and rulings - the general theory of civil rulings in our Islamic jurisprudence will emerge complete, standing on its feet, well-established, well-knit, similar to its known method today in modern legal jurisprudence, with two great important introductions.
Then in the parts following those four of the series, we research the named contracts arranged and treated in the modern subject-based style, starting from the contract of sale, so the jurisprudential applications of those principles and general rulings in private contracts also appear.
I have committed myself to attributing rulings to their original sources and locations in the books of jurisprudence, except what is well-known and does not need attribution, so that the reader can refer to those jurisprudential sources and this new style does not make the student unable to refer back to the original jurisprudential source in its old fundamental books and benefit from it.
And this innovative work in the method of presenting Islamic jurisprudence by moving from the branch form to the subject form requires the collaborative efforts of knowledgeable professors and scholars acquainted with Islamic jurisprudence and with a group of modern law in its foreign sources and methods, and it also requires time for thorough consideration in planning, comprehensive coverage, arrangement, refinement, and revision.
=
- The harmful act from the sources of obligation mentioned above. I published at its end the texts of the general theory complete (the preliminary chapter, sources of obligation, then rulings up to extinction of obligation and its causes).
Then the Legal Department stopped following up on the draft unified civil code fully rooted on Islamic jurisprudence in the ideal form with which we set up the general theory. Therefore, I then published the texts of the general theory at the end of my mentioned book (al-Fi'l al-Darr) so that the fruits of this glorious work would not be lost.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (Khatbat al-Kitab) to his series 'Islamic Jurisprudence in Its New Garment'. He describes the structure of the series: Parts 1 and 2 constitute the 'General Introduction to Fiqh' (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm), Part 3 introduces the general theory of civil rulings, and Part 4 was intended to present that general theory modeled after foreign theories of obligations. However, Part 4 was never published due to the Syrian Civil Code replacing the Majalla; the author later published parts of it separately. He also mentions his involvement in a League of Arab States committee to draft a unified Arab civil code based on Islamic fiqh. The author emphasizes his methodology of citing original sources, aiming to bridge classical fiqh and modern legal science. For students of Usul al-Ifta’, this front matter shows the author's reformist approach to presenting fiqh in a modern, subject-based arrangement while maintaining fidelity to classical sources.

Key Terms
المدخل الفقهي العام (al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm): The first two parts of the series, providing a general introduction to Islamic jurisprudence.
النظرية العامة للأحكام المدنية (al-Nazariyyah al-‘Ammah li al-Ahkam al-Madaniyyah): The general theory of civil rulings, a comprehensive framework for rights, obligations, property, and persons in Islamic law.
الشكل الفروعي (al-shakl al-furū'ī): The traditional branch-based arrangement of fiqh, organized by specific topics such as purity, prayer, sale, etc.
الشكل الموضوعي (al-shakl al-mawḍū'ī): The subject-based arrangement, grouping rulings under theoretical categories like rights, obligations, and property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many parts are planned for the series 'الفقه الإسلامي في ثوبه الجديد'?
A) Two parts
B) Three parts
C) Four parts
D) Five parts

Key Takeaway
Al-Zarqa's series represents a methodological shift: presenting fiqh in a subject-based, theory-driven framework while maintaining strict source attribution to classical texts.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
Al-Zarqa's Methodological Shift in Presenting Fiqh
Author's preface outlining the methodology and scope of the series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the methodological shift from branch-based (furu‘i) to subject-based (mawdu‘i) presentation of Islamic fiqh and identify the author's approach to citing sources.

Core Idea
Al-Zarqa's 'objective form' reorganizes fiqh by legal subject, not by branch, making it accessible to modern legal minds.

In this preface, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a prominent 20th-century Hanafi jurist, introduces his landmark work *Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm*. He outlines a revolutionary methodology: presenting Islamic civil law not through the traditional fiqh chapters but by legal concepts and categories akin to modern civil codes. This lesson unpacks his vision, his commitment to source attribution, and the collaborative nature of this project.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shift
Recognize the transition from الشكل الفروعي (branch form) to الشكل الموضوعي (objective form). The author explicitly states he is moving away from the traditional branch-based arrangement.
2
Locate the structure
Note the four-part plan for the general theory of civil rulings, followed by specific contracts like sale. This is a subject-based organization.
3
Examine source methodology
Understand his principle of عزو الأحكام إلى مصادرها الأصلية (attributing rulings to original sources) to preserve access to classical texts.
4
Reflect on collaboration
Realize the need for combined expertise in fiqh and modern law. The author acknowledges that such a work requires the efforts of many scholars.
5
Acknowledge the context
Appreciate the timing: the third edition incorporates new works and the Syrian Civil Code. This shows the dynamic nature of the project.

خطبة الكتاب
وبتمام هذه الأجزاء الأربعة إن شاء الله - وهي تتضمن المباحث والأحكام العامة - تبرز النظرية العامة للأحكام المدنية في فقهنا الإسلامي كاملة، وتقوم على قدميها موطأة الأكناف محبوكة الأطراف، بمثل طريقتها المعروفة اليوم في الفقه القانوني الحديث مع مَدْخَلَيْن عظيمين هامين .
ثم في الأجزاء التي تتلو تلك الأربعة من السلسلة، نبحث عن العقود المسماة مرتبة ومعالجة بالأسلوب الموضوعي الحديث مبتدئين من عقد : البيع، فتظهر أيضاً التطبيقات الفقهية لتلك المبادىء والأحكام العامة في العقود الخاصة .
وقد أخذت نفسي بعزو الأحكام إلى مصادرها الأصلية ومواقعها من كتب الفقه إلا ما كان مشهوراً غير محتاج إلى عزو، وذلك لكي يتمكن القارىء من الرجوع إلى تلك المصادر الفقهية ولا يكون في هذا الأسلوب الجديد ما يجعل الطالب غير قادر على الرجوع إلى المنبع الفقهي في كتبه القديمة الأساسية، والاستفادة منه .
وإن هذا العمل التجديدي في طريقة عرض الفقه الإسلامي بالانتقال من الشكل الفروعي إلى الشكل الموضوعي يقتضي أن تتعاون عليه جهود الأساتذة العلماء المطلعين على الفقه الإسلامي وعلى علم القانون الحديث في مصادره وأساليبه الأجنبية كما يقتضي من الوقت ما يروي الروية في التخطيط والإحاطة والترتيب والتهذيب والتنقيح.

Preface of the Book
With the completion of these four parts, God willing — which contain the general topics and rulings — the general theory of civil rulings in our Islamic fiqh will emerge complete, standing firm, well-settled, coherent, in a manner similar to its known method today in modern legal fiqh, with two great important introductions.
Then in the parts that follow these four of the series, we research the named contracts arranged and treated in the modern objective style, starting with the contract of sale; thus the jurisprudential applications of those general principles and rulings in specific contracts will also appear.
I have taken it upon myself to attribute rulings to their original sources and locations in the books of fiqh, except what is well-known and does not need attribution, so that the reader can refer back to those jurisprudential sources and that this new style does not make the student unable to refer to the jurisprudential source in its basic old books and benefit from it.
And this renewal work in the method of presenting Islamic fiqh by moving from the branch-based form to the objective form requires that the efforts of scholar professors knowledgeable in Islamic fiqh and modern law in its foreign sources and methods cooperate in it, and it also requires time that satisfies deliberation in planning, comprehensiveness, arrangement, refinement, and revision.
=
- The harmful act from the sources of obligation mentioned above. And I published at the end of it the texts of the general theory complete (the introductory chapter and sources of obligation, then rulings until the extinction of the obligation and its causes of extinction).
Then the legal circle stopped following up the project of the unified civil code, completely grounded on Islamic fiqh in the ideal form with which we placed the general theory. Therefore, I then published the texts of the general theory at the end of my aforementioned book (The Harmful Act) so that the fruits of this glorious work would not be lost.
32

Preface of the Book
And this work is too great and magnificent for an individual to undertake, but the urgent need for speed and racing against time decreed that I proceed to bear this heavy burden that would tire even the strong, so I combine preparation, delivery, writing, and production in a practical beginning as much as my capacity in all parts of the book at one time, to meet the need of the three classes together in the Syrian Faculty of Law.
From God Almighty I seek help, and upon God is the direction of the way, and He is the best trustee (1).
Damascus, 8 Safar 1354 AH
22 January 1945 CE
Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa
(1) We needed to reprint this introduction before its completion until it was completed in the third edition. In this edition we have transferred from modern books that appeared after the second edition of this book, or appeared shortly before, but I came across them recently, such as the book 'Abu Hanifa', the book 'Malik', the book 'Ibn Hanbal', the book 'Property and the Theory of Contract in Islamic Sharia' by the investigative professor Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, and the book 'Right and Liability' by the esteemed professor Sheikh Ali al-Khafif, both of whom are professors of Islamic Sharia in the Faculty of Law at Cairo University, and the book 'The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Sharia' by the lawyer professor Dr. Subhi Mahmasani, professor of the journal and Roman law at the American University of Beirut.
Moreover, since the Syrian Civil Code was issued during the third edition as well, we have quoted from it and attributed things to it that were issued in 1949 CE after the writing of this book, as was previously indicated in the introduction to the third edition.
33
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface (خطبة الكتاب) to his work *Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm*. Here, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa outlines the structure and methodology of his multi-volume series. He explains that the first four parts will present the general theory of civil rulings in Islamic fiqh, organized thematically (الشكل الموضوعي) rather than by traditional furu‘ branches. Subsequent parts will treat named contracts like sale, applying the general principles. The author emphasizes his commitment to citing original fiqh sources (عزو الأحكام إلى مصادرها الأصلية) to allow readers to connect with classical texts. He acknowledges that this reformative approach requires collaborative effort from scholars versed in both Islamic fiqh and modern legal methods. The footnote added in the third edition mentions recent works he consulted, including those by Muhammad Abu Zahra, Ali al-Khafif, and Subhi Mahmasani, as well as the Syrian Civil Code (1949). This front matter sets the stage for the entire work, explaining its purpose and the author's scholarly methodology.

Key Terms
النظرية العامة للأحكام المدنية (al-nazariyyah al-‘ammah li-l-ahkam al-madaniyyah): The general theory of civil rulings, a comprehensive framework for organizing Islamic legal rules concerning civil obligations, property, and contracts in a systematic manner.
الشكل الموضوعي (al-shakl al-mawdu‘i): The objective/subject-based form of presenting fiqh, organizing topics by legal subject matter rather than by branches (فروع).

Understanding the Author's Preface in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm
Author explains his motivation and updates in later editions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of an author's preface in a fiqh textbook and identify key elements of scholarly methodology reflected in Mustafa al-Zarqa's preface.

Core Idea
An author's preface reveals scholarly intention, methodology, and intellectual context — it is a gateway to understanding the text.

This lesson examines the preface (خطبة الكتاب) from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's seminal work, al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. Though not a legal ruling, the preface provides crucial insight into the author's motivation, the academic context, and his approach to updating later editions. For a student of Hanafi fiqh and iftaa methodology, understanding such front matter is essential for situating a text within its scholarly tradition and evaluating its sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated purpose
Read the opening lines: al-Zarqa says the work is too great for one person but urgent need forced him. What specific need does he mention?
The need of 'three classes together in the Syrian Faculty of Law'.
2
Analyze the author's methodological approach
Notice how al-Zarqa describes combining multiple roles: preparation, delivery, editing, and production. What does this tell us about his working method?
It shows he was both scholar and publisher, hastening to meet academic demands.
3
Examine the footnote on later editions
In the long footnote, al-Zarqa lists new books and references added in the third edition. Identify the types of sources he cites.
Includes biographies (Abu Hanifa, Malik, Ibn Hanbal), substantive works (ownership, contract theory, obligations), and the Syrian Civil Code.
4
Reflect on the significance for contemporary fiqh studies
Consider how this preface demonstrates the living nature of fiqh: later editions incorporate new scholarship and legal developments. How should a mufti use this information?
Always check the preface of the edition you are using to understand updates and corrections.

(1) We needed to renew the printing of this introduction before its completion until it was finished in the third edition. We have included in this edition from modern books that appeared after the second edition of this book, or appeared shortly before, but I have only recently seen them, such as the book 'Abu Hanifa', the book 'Malik', the book 'Ibn Hanbal', the book 'Ownership and the Theory of Contract in Islamic Law' by the investigating professor Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra, and the book 'Right and Liability' by the venerable professor Shaykh Ali al-Khafif, both of whom are professors of Islamic law in the Faculty of Law at Cairo University, and the book 'The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Law' by the lawyer professor Dr. Subhi Mahmassani, professor of the Journal and Roman Law at the American University of Beirut. Commentary
This passage is the author's preface (خطبة الكتاب) from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. The author explains that producing a comprehensive introduction to Islamic jurisprudence is a monumental task, but he took it upon himself due to urgent academic necessity, specifically to meet the needs of students in the Syrian Faculty of Law. He combined multiple roles—preparer, lecturer, editor, and publisher—to expedite the work. The date indicates the original preface was written in early 1945. The lengthy footnote, added later for the third edition, provides valuable insight into the author's methodology: he updated the work by incorporating recently published, important scholarly texts, such as works by Muhammad Abu Zahra on Abu Hanifa and contract theory, Ali al-Khafif on rights and liability, and Subhi Mahmassani on obligations and contracts. He also notes the incorporation of references to the Syrian Civil Code (1949). This transparency about sources and editions reflects scholarly integrity and the living nature of fiqh scholarship, where later editions build upon earlier ones with new legal developments.

Key Terms
خطبة الكتاب (khuṭbat al-kitāb): The author's introductory preface to the book, explaining its purpose and background.

Introduction and Front Matter

Book Division and Reference Conventions
Introduction to book division and reference symbols.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three-level hierarchy of the book (fasl, bab, qism) and interpret the cross-reference symbols (Quran citation numbers and the letter ra).

Core Idea
This book uses sequential numbering for chapters, sections, and parts — making cross-referencing simpler than nested divisions.

Before diving into the legal content, Al-Zarqa introduces the structure of his book. Understanding this system will allow you to navigate the work efficiently, locate any topic by its chapter number, and correctly interpret the reference abbreviations used throughout.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three levels
Find the Arabic terms for the three hierarchical divisions: chapter (fasl), section (bab), part (qism). Note that each is a group of the previous level.
In fiqh works, 'bab' is often a major topical section; 'fasl' is a smaller unit.
2
Trace the numbering system
Notice that both chapters (fusuul) and sections (abwaab) are numbered consecutively from start to end. This allows precise cross-referencing.
Example: the first section (bab) may contain chapters 1-3, the next begins at chapter 4.
3
Learn the Quran citation format
When a Quranic verse is cited, two numbers follow the surah name: the first is the surah number, the second the verse number. If only one number, it is the verse number (the surah is implied or mentioned).
This is a standard convention in many Arabic scholarly works.
4
Recognize the cross-reference symbol
The solitary letter 'ر' is an abbreviation for 'unzur' (look, see). It directs the reader to another part of the book.
It is not 'ra'aytu' (I saw) nor 'riwayah' (narration).

Divisions of this book, its symbols and conventions. In this new arrangement, the basic small units composing the book are chapters (fusuul). Every group of chapters of related subject matter forms a section (bauwab), and every group of sections of related subject matter forms a part (qism). The chapters have continuous sequential numbers from the beginning of the book to its end. For example, the first introductory section includes chapters (1)-(3). Then follows the second section which begins with chapter /4/ and ends with chapter //, after which the third section begins with chapter /9/. And so on. Likewise, the sections have continuous sequential numbers from the beginning of the book to its end. The first part of the book ends with the fourth section, after which the second part begins from the fifth section... and so on. This modern method of division is much easier for the reader and simpler for cross-referencing than the descending subdividing method used in all previous editions of the Madhkal. We have also made the text segments and research stations detailed and distinguished by paragraphs with sequential numbers within each chapter to assist the reader and facilitate referencing and review. We have used in citation and cross-reference conventions which we explain below: 1 - The two numbers after the name of a Quranic surah: the first is for the surah, the second for the ayah therein. If only one number follows the surah name, it is for the ayah. 2 - The single letter ra is the imperative verb from ra'aya (to see), meaning: see (i.e., refer to).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the structure and conventions of the book. The author explains that the book is now divided into a clear hierarchy: chapters (fusuul) are the smallest units, grouped into sections (abwaab), which are grouped into parts (aqsaam). All chapters and sections are numbered consecutively throughout the book, making it easy for the reader to locate content and for the author to cross-reference. This is a departure from earlier editions which used a nested subdivision system. Additionally, the author explains the reference notation: when citing Quranic verses, two numbers after the surah name refer to surah and ayah; if only one number, it is the ayah. The solitary letter 'ر' is an abbreviation for 'unzur' (look), indicating a cross-reference. This is standard convention in many Arabic scholarly works.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): the smallest basic unit of the book; a chapter
باب (bab): a group of chapters with related subject matter; a section
قسم (qism): a group of sections; a part of the book
حرف الراء (harf al-ra'): the single letter ra used as an abbreviation for 'unzur' (see/refer to)

Understanding the Book's Divisions and Citation Conventions
Explains book's structure and citation symbols

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the citation symbols (R, F, M, J, S, QM) used in Hanafi legal texts and locate any referenced passage using the book's division system.

Core Idea
Every symbol in a citation tells you exactly where to look—if you know the code.

Legal texts like al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm use a consistent system of divisions and symbols to make cross-referencing efficient. This lesson unpacks that system so you can navigate and cite Hanafi sources with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-tier division
Recognize that the book is divided into chapters (fusul), sections (abwab), and parts (aqsam), each numbered consecutively.
For example, the first part includes sections 1–4, and within each section, chapters are numbered continuously.
2
Match the symbols to their meanings
Match each single-letter symbol (R, F, M, J, S, QM) to its referent: look, chapter, article, volume, page, Syrian Civil Code.
Remember: 'F' is for fasl (chapter), 'M' for maddah (article), 'J' for juz' (volume), 'S' for safhah (page).
3
Parse a compound citation
Break down a citation like (ر: ف ٦/١٧) into components: R (see), F (chapter), 6/17 (paragraph 6 of chapter 17).
The first number after 'F' is the paragraph, the second is the chapter number.
4
Interpret range and edition notations
Understand that a horizontal line between numbers (e.g., 16-20) indicates inclusive range, and that specific editions are assumed for frequently cited works.
For example, 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' defaults to the first large-format Amiriya edition.

٣٦
Divisions of this book, its symbols and conventions

In this new arrangement, the basic small units that make up the book are the chapters. Each group of chapters with a related subject forms a section, and each group of sections with a related subject forms a part.
The chapters have consecutive numbers from the beginning of the book to the end. For example, the first introductory section includes chapters (1) to (3). It is followed by the second section beginning with chapter /4/ and ending with chapter //, then the third section begins with chapter /9/. And so on.
Similarly, the sections have consecutive numbers from the beginning of the book to the end. The first part of the book ends with the fourth section, then the second part begins with the fifth section, and so on.
This modern method of division is much easier for the reader, and simpler for reference than the descending, overlapping hierarchical method used in all previous editions of al-Madkhal.
We have made the paragraphs of speech and research stations detailed and distinguished by numbered sequences within a single chapter to assist the reader and facilitate reference and review.
We have used in citation and reference conventions that we explain below:
1. The two numbers after the name of a Quranic surah: the first is for the surah, and the second for the verse in it. If there is a single number after the surah name, it is for the verse.
2. The letter 'R' alone is an imperative verb from 'ru'ya' meaning: look.
3. The letter 'F' followed by two numbers is a symbol for the chapter: the first of the two numbers refers to the chapter, and the second number to the intended paragraph in it. Example: (R: F 6/17 meaning: look at chapter seventeen - paragraph 6 of it).
4. The letter 'M' preceded by the name of a book with sequential articles, such as the Majalla, indicates the article with that number from the mentioned book. In this case we may dispense with the 'M', such as (R) al-Majalla (270), meaning view article 270 of the Majalla.
5. The letter 'J' (ج) is a symbol for the volume of a multi-volume work, and the letter 'S' (ص) is a symbol for the page. We may suffice with mentioning the volume number and page number only, separated by a slash, e.g., (al-Bada'i 5/67: the first number is for the volume, the second for the page. If the book is a single volume, the number after it is for the page.
6. The letter 'Q' with 'M' (قم) followed by a number is a symbol for the new Syrian Civil Code, and the number is for the intended article.
7. If we mention in citation and reference to articles or pages or paragraphs two numbers with a horizontal line between them, e.g., (16-20), the intention is that the reference includes all numbers between the two mentioned numbers in a series.
8. Wherever we cite a page from the book al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss Rad al-Muhtar without specifying the edition, the intended is the first Amiriya edition with the large format. And if we cite from Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, the intended is the Kastiliyya edition of the year 1280 AH. And if we cite from the book al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, the intended is the Istanbul edition.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is the front matter explaining the book's structure and citation conventions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, describes a modernized division of the book into parts (aqsam), sections (abwab), and chapters (fusul), with consecutive numbering throughout the work. He explains that each chapter consists of numbered paragraphs (faqarat) to aid cross-referencing. The remainder of the passage details the notation system used for citations: 'R' (ra') means 'see'; 'F' (fa') followed by two numbers refers to chapter and paragraph; 'M' (mim) before a book's name indicates an article number; 'J' (jim) for volume and 'S' (sad) for page; 'QM' (qaf-mim) for the Syrian Civil Code; and a horizontal line between numbers indicates a range. The passage also specifies which editions of key Hanafi works are cited (al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Rad al-Muhtar, Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, and al-Durar). This is purely methodological and not a legal ruling.

Key Terms
الفصل (al-fasl): The basic unit of the book, sequentially numbered from beginning to end, containing a complete subtopic.
الباب (al-bab): A group of chapters with related subjects, also sequentially numbered throughout the book.
القسم (al-qism): A major division of the book composed of multiple sections (abwab), also sequentially numbered.
حرف الراء (ر) (harf al-ra'): Symbol for 'see' (an imperative from ru'ya), used to cross-reference.
حرف الفاء (ف) (harf al-fa'): Symbol for chapter (fasl), followed by two numbers: first for chapter, second for paragraph.
حرف الميم (م) (harf al-mim): Symbol for article (maddah) in a codified text like the Majalla.
حرف الجيم (ج) وحرف الصاد (ص) (harf al-jim wa al-sad): Symbols for volume (juz') and page (safhah) respectively.
حرف القاف مع الميم (قم) (harf al-qaf ma'a al-mim): Symbol for the new Syrian Civil Code (al-Qanun al-Madani al-Suri al-Jadid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter

Decoding al-Zarqa's Citation Symbols and Conventions
Explaining the book's reference symbols and citation conventions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each citation symbol (ف, م, ج, ص, قم) used in al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm and correctly interpret a compound citation string.

Core Idea
Every symbol in a citation unlocks a specific reference: chapter, article, volume, or page.

This lesson introduces the standard reference symbols used throughout al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. Rather than containing fiqh rulings, this passage equips you with the navigational tools to find the actual legal content efficiently. Mastering these conventions is essential for independent study and for correctly attributing citations in your own writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify each symbol
Scan the citation string for ف, م, ج, ص, قم. Note that some symbols may be omitted (like م when the book name is clear).
Symbols are always followed by numbers.
2
Match symbol to meaning
Recall the meaning: ف = chapter (fasl), م = article (madda), ج = volume (juz'), ص = page (sahifa), قم = Syrian Civil Code article.
ج and ص often appear together as ج/ص.
3
Determine the numbers
For ف, the first number is the chapter, the second is the paragraph. For م, the number is the article. For ج/ص, the first number is volume, second is page. For قم, the number is the article.
If the book is single-volume, a lone number after the book name refers to the page.
4
Handle ranges and repeated citations
If two numbers are separated by a horizontal line (e.g., 50-55), it means all numbers in that range. The word 'aydan' (أيضاً) after a book name means the same volume and page as the previous citation of that book.
Ranges are inclusive.
5
Apply edition defaults
Some books have specified editions: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar use the first Amiriya edition (large size). Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya uses the Kastelli 1280 AH edition. Al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar uses the Istanbul edition. If no edition is stated, these defaults apply.
Footnotes marked with * are optional for advanced study.

٣٧
*
Divisions of the book and its symbols and terminologies
3 - The letter Faa followed by two numbers is a symbol for the chapter, then the first of the two numbers refers to the chapter, and the second number for the intended paragraph therein.
Example: (R: F 6/17 means see chapter seventeen - paragraph 6 thereof).
4 - The letter Meem preceded by the name of a book having serial articles like Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah refers to the article with that number from the mentioned book. In this case we may dispense with the Meem, such as (R) al-Majallah (270), i.e., see article 270 of Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah.
5 - The letter Jeem (J) is a symbol for the volume of a book having multiple volumes, and the letter Sad (S) is a symbol for the page. We may suffice with mentioning only the volume number and page number separated by a slash like: (al-Bada'i' 5/67: the first number is for the volume, the second for the page. If the book is a single volume, the number after it is for the page.
6 - The letter Qaaf with Meem (Qm) followed by a number is a symbol for the new Syrian Civil Code, and the number is for the intended article thereof.
7 - If we mention in citations and references to articles or pages or paragraphs two numbers with a horizontal line between them, like e.g.: (16-20), then the reference is intended to include also what is between the two mentioned numbers of serial numbers.
8 - Wherever we cite to a page from the book al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss Radd al-Muhtar without specifying the edition, then the intended is the first Amiriya edition of large size.
And if we cite to Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, the intended is the Kastelli edition of the year 1280 AH.
And if we cite to the book al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, the intended is the Istanbul edition.
36
Divisions of the book and its symbols and terminologies
9 - When repeating a citation to a book after a short interval, if we place after the name of the book the word (also), then its meaning is the same volume and page cited from it before.
(Example) - If we say for example: (R: J 3 F /27/ and al-Majallah/250/ and Qanun al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya M/50-55/ and Radd al-Muhtar 3/200/ and Qm/384) then its meaning is: see paragraph 27 from the third volume of this book and article 250 of al-Majallah, and articles 50 to 55 of the Personal Status Law, and page 200 of volume 3 of Radd al-Muhtar, and article 384 of the Syrian Civil Code.
10 - Footnotes marked with an asterisk (*) next to their number are not required from students; rather they are for those who wish to delve deeper, similar to appendices at the ends of chapters.
*
*
37
*
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is front matter from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm. It explains the book's internal reference system and symbols used in citations. The author systematically defines each symbol: 'f' for chapter (fasl), 'm' for article (madda) in codes like the Majalla, 'j' for volume (juz'), and 's' for page (sahifa). He also specifies editions for key Hanafi texts: al-Durr al-Mukhtar with Radd al-Muhtar (first Amiriya edition), Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya (Kastelli 1280 AH), and al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar (Istanbul edition). The passage also explains how repeated citations are simplified with 'aydan' (also) and that footnotes marked with an asterisk are optional for advanced study. This is purely procedural, not legal content, but essential for navigating the book.

Key Terms
ف (F): Symbol for chapter (fasl) followed by two numbers: first for chapter number, second for paragraph number.
م (M): Symbol for article (madda) in a codified law like the Majalla.
ج (J): Symbol for volume (juz') of a multi-volume work.
ص (S): Symbol for page (sahifa).
قم (Qm): Symbol for the new Syrian Civil Code (al-Qanun al-Madani al-Suri al-Jadid).
أيضاً (aydan): Used after a book name in repeated citation to mean the same volume and page as the previous citation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The paragraph number
C) The volume number
D) The page number

Key Takeaway
Mastering al-Zarqa's citation symbols is the key to efficiently navigating al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm and its references to Hanafi primary sources.

Navigating Book Citations and Footnotes
Front matter explaining citation conventions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify repeated citations using 'أيضاً' and distinguish optional asterisk footnotes in a Hanafi legal text.

Core Idea
Efficient citation reading: 'أيضاً' means same source reference; asterisk footnotes are optional deep dives.

When studying a detailed fiqh text, you will encounter citations to multiple sources. The author explains two key conventions: how to interpret repeated citations and which footnotes are essential. Mastering these will save you time and prevent confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the citation pattern
Look for the word 'أيضاً' after a book name. It signals that the volume and page reference is the same as the immediately preceding citation of that book.
Example: after citing 'Radd al-Muhtar', if you see 'Radd al-Muhtar also', the page reference is unchanged.
2
Parse the example citation string
The author gives a sample citation string. Break it down: each source is separated by a slash. Note the abbreviations and number formats: 'ر' for the book itself, 'المجلة' for al-Majalla, etc.
Practice reading the string aloud to internalize the structure.
3
Distinguish required vs. optional material
Footnotes with an asterisk (*) are not required for basic study. They are for deeper research, like appendices. Focus on unmarked footnotes first.
This helps manage reading load.
4
Apply to your own reading
When you encounter a citation with 'أيضاً', check the prior citation. For asterisk footnotes, decide if you need them now or can return later.
This skill transfers to any well-referenced Hanafi text.

Divisions of the book, its symbols, and its terminology. 9 - When repeating a citation to a book after a short interval, if we place after the book's name the word (also), then it means the same part and page from it as before. (Example) - If for example it is said: (R) vol. 3, p./27/ and al-Majalla/250/ and the Personal Status Law art./50-55/ and Radd al-Muhtar 200/3/ and QM/384), then its meaning is: see paragraph /27/ from part three of this book and article /250/ from al-Majalla, and articles 50 to 55 of the Personal Status Law, and page 200 of part 3 of Radd al-Muhtar, and article /384/ of the Syrian Civil Code. 10 - Footnotes distinguished by an asterisk (*) next to their number are not required from students; rather they are for those who wish to delve deeper, similar to the appendices at the ends of chapters.

Commentary
This passage is from the front matter of the book, where the author explains the system of citations and notation used throughout. It contains two points. First, when a citation to the same book is repeated shortly after the first mention, the author uses the word 'also' (أيضاً) to indicate that the reference is to the same volume and page as previously cited. The author provides an example showing a string of citations to various sources: his own book, al-Majalla, the Personal Status Law, Radd al-Muhtar, and the Syrian Civil Code. Second, the author clarifies that footnotes marked with an asterisk (*) are not required reading for students; they are intended for those who wish to study more deeply, similar to appendices at the end of chapters. This is a practical guide for navigating the book's references.

Key Terms
عزو (‘azw): Citation or attribution of a reference to a source.
حاشية (hāshiyah): A footnote or marginal note; here specifically those marked with an asterisk.
أيضاً (ayḍan): In this context, indicates a repeated citation to the same book, same volume and page as previously cited.

Key Takeaway

The Scope of Part One: Fiqh Foundations
Title heading for Part One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and purpose of the first part of this text and explain why a definitional and historical introduction is foundational to usul al-iftaa.

Core Idea
The study of Islamic jurisprudence begins with defining its scope and tracing its historical roots.

This lesson introduces a major section heading that frames the entire first part of our text. In the Hanafi usul al-iftaa tradition, such introductory material is not merely decorative—it establishes the epistemological basis upon which all subsequent legal reasoning rests. Understanding what this part covers prepares you to engage with the detailed discussions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heading
Read the Arabic heading carefully. Note that it announces a 'muqaddimah ta'rifiyya wa tarikhiyya' (an introductory and historical introduction). This tells you the author intends to first define the subject (fiqh) and then trace its development through history.
The term muqaddimah here signals a foundational chapter.
2
Understand the purpose
Consider why a book on usul al-iftaa—the science of fatwa-giving—would begin with definitions and history. The answer: a mufti must know what fiqh is (its definition, sources, and scope) and how it evolved, to correctly apply rulings in new contexts.
Usul al-iftaa requires historical awareness to distinguish between primary and secondary rulings.
3
Relate to Hanafi method
In the Hanafi school, the historical development (tatawwur) of fiqh is crucial because it explains why certain rulings were given in specific eras and how later jurists (mutakhkhirin) relied on earlier authorities. This introduction sets the stage for understanding the hierarchical authority of texts like Zahir al-Riwayah.
This aligns with the iftaa discipline's emphasis on tarjih based on historical layers.

Title and outline of the first chapter

A fiqh methodology text begins with defining terms and mapping the field before any rulings.

The outline then breaks the chapter into two sections: a general overview of Islamic Sharia, and then the meaning of fiqh with its categories of rulings. Understanding this macro structure helps you see the author's pedagogical plan: first situate fiqh within the broader Sharia, then define and classify its subject matter.

Guided Path

The Comprehensive Scope of Islamic Sharia
Book introduction outline

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main divisions of Islamic law (private and public rights) and list the subcategories within each as outlined by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Islamic law comprehensively governs both private and public spheres, integrating divine and human dimensions.

Before diving into the details of Hanafi fiqh, it is essential to see the full landscape of Islamic Sharia. This lesson orients you to the structure of a foundational textbook, revealing how Islamic law covers everything from family and transactions to constitutional and international matters, all grounded in divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main sections of the outline
The outline divides Islamic law into 'Private Rights' (al-Ḥuqūq al-Khāṣṣah) and 'Public Rights' (al-Ḥuqūq al-ʿĀmmah). Identify these as the broadest categories.
This division reflects a systematic approach to organizing fiqh topics.
2
List the subcategories under Private Rights
Under Private Rights, the outline lists: family rights (personal status), marriage/dissolution, guardianship, inheritance, civil transactions, and criminal law. Note how criminal law is placed under private rights – a distinctive classification.
In Hanafi fiqh, crimes are often treated as violations of private rights, though they also involve public aspects.
3
List the subcategories under Public Rights
Public Rights include internal rights (constitutional, administrative, public finance) and external rights (international law). Observe that public finance is considered a public right.
This anticipates later discussions of state and treasury in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Relate the outline to the sources of law
The passage begins with a comparison of positive laws (man-made) and divine laws. Recognize that Islamic Sharia is presented as divine, with its legal system derived from the Qur'an, Sunnah, ijma', and qiyas.
This sets the epistemological foundation for Hanafi usul.
5
Note the author's intention
This outline is a preface to a jurisprudential series. Understand that the author aims to provide a comprehensive overview before detailing specific rulings.
As future muftis, grasping this scope helps contextualize the rulings you encounter.

Source Text

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Three Pillars of Islamic Sharia
Introduces the three pillars of Islam and focuses on the legal system

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three pillars of Islamic Sharia and explain the scope of its legal system.

Core Idea
Islamic Sharia is not just law; it integrates creed, worship, and a legal-judicial system.

This introduction clarifies the comprehensive nature of Islamic Sharia, which is often reduced to its legal dimension. The author emphasizes that Sharia rests on three interconnected pillars: rational creed, spiritual worship, and a legal judicial system. Understanding this tripartite structure is essential for anyone seeking to study the Hanafi legal tradition within its broader Islamic context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Pillars
Read the passage and extract the three pillars on which Islamic Sharia is built. Note that they are: 'aqida 'aqliyya (rational creed), 'ibada ruhiyya (spiritual worship), and nizam qanuni qada'i (legal judicial system).
The third pillar is the focus of this fiqh series.
2
Understand the Scope of the Legal System
The passage divides the legal system into private rights (civil and criminal) and public rights (constitutional, administrative, financial, international). Recognize that these categories are foundational for Hanafi fiqh classifications.
Private rights include family law (al-ahwal al-shakhsiyya), which is detailed later.
3
Distinguish System from Application
The author warns against confusing the ideal Sharia system with its historical application. Misunderstandings or misapplications do not invalidate the system itself.
This principle is crucial for a mufti: rulings are based on the system, not on corrupted practices.
4
Trace the Development of Fiqh Schools
The passage notes that fiqh evolved through ijtihad based on Quran and Sunnah, resulting in multiple schools (madhahib). The differences are legal, not religious.
The Hanafi school is the first of the four living schools mentioned.

Chapter (1) General Overview of Islamic Sharia
...and the punishment in the Hereafter so that the believer is in constant watch over his deeds, careful not to fall short in his duties.
c) Reforming society, i.e., social life in a manner where order, public security, justice among people, safeguarding reasonable freedoms and human dignity prevail.
To achieve this last social goal, Islam brought a civil system that includes comprehensive legislation of the legal foundations necessary to establish a collective social life in a state, organizing the relationships of people with each other, their relationships with the ruling authority, and safeguarding the private rights of individuals and the public rights of the community. From these three goals of Islam, the meaning of Islamic Sharia is determined, and it becomes clear that it rests on three pillars: rational creed, spiritual worship, and a legal judicial system. This is the intended meaning when it is said that Islam is a religion and a state.
Moreover, one must distinguish between the system and its application; for it cannot be denied that in practical and historical reality, the Islamic truth is often misunderstood in some of these three aspects, or misapplied, resulting in a distorted image. Indeed, this third aspect, which is the legal system of Islamic Sharia, is alone the intended subject here in this fiqh series, the series 'Islamic Fiqh in its New Garment'.
The Legal System in Islamic Sharia
8/1 - Indeed, the legal judicial system in the Sharia encompassed fundamental rules and rulings in both fields: the field of private rights with its civil and criminal branches, and the field of public rights with its internal and external branches, i.e., constitutional, administrative, financial, and international.

Chapter One: Introduction, Definition, and Classification
These principles were brought by the original texts of Islamic Sharia in all of the Quran and the Prophetic Sunnah as basic principles, leaving details to ijtihad in application according to temporal interests and spatial possibilities, except for a few rulings that were addressed in detail, such as inheritance rulings and some penalties.
Around those legal principles in their various fields, and as a result of application in Islamic lands where Muslims encountered the effects of ancient civilizations, and as a result of the evolution of different economic circumstances, a great interpretive and detailed fiqh emerged around the original texts of the Sharia—the Book and the Prophetic Sunnah—through the ijtihad of the jurist-explainers and the ruling judges. This Islamic fiqh was the greatest and most extensive legal fiqh known to date in the history of legal systems. Many fiqh schools emerged within it, the most famous being the four living schools today: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. The difference between these schools is not religious; rather, it is a legal-judicial difference. This difference gave rise to a great legislative wealth in the theories of Islamic fiqh.
-
The Most Important Basic Principles
In this Legal System of Islamic Sharia: First Requirement
Section of Private Rights
a) Family Rights (Personal Status)
9/1 - In its legal section, Islamic Sharia brought a comprehensive system organizing all situations of the family and its relationships, today called personal status (Statuts personnels). It began first by rescuing women from the degraded position they had within their families, for women did not enjoy the right to life, let alone other rights. Thus, the Quran forbade the killing of daughters that was customary in the Arab Jahiliyyah, and the Quran denounced it.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a general introduction to Islamic Sharia, outlining its three foundational pillars: creed (`aqida), worship (`ibada), and a legal-judicial system (nizam qanuni qada'i). The author emphasizes that the legal system is the focus of this fiqh series. He distinguishes between the ideal system and its historical application, warning against judging Islam by distorted practices. The passage then describes the scope of the legal system, covering private and public rights, and explains how Islamic fiqh developed through ijtihad, resulting in the four Sunni schools of law. Finally, it introduces family law as the first part of private rights, highlighting Islam's reform of women's status by prohibiting female infanticide. As a Hanafi work, this text sets the stage for detailed study of Hanafi fiqh within the broader framework of Sharia.

Key Terms
الشريعة الإسلامية (al-Shari'a al-Islamiyya): The divine law of Islam, encompassing creed, worship, and a legal system.
النظام القانوني القضائي (al-nizam al-qanuni al-qada'i): The legal and judicial system of Sharia, covering rules for private and public rights.
الأحوال الشخصية (al-ahwal al-shakhsiyya): Personal status, i.e., family law matters including marriage, divorce, inheritance, and custody.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma

Key Takeaway
Islamic Sharia is a comprehensive divine system integrating creed, worship, and law — understanding this foundation is essential before delving into Hanafi fiqh.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence: Primary and Secondary
Listing the chapters on primary and secondary sources of fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the primary and secondary sources of fiqh and identify their order as per the Hanafi classification.

Core Idea
The sources of Islamic law are divided into primary (Qur’an, Sunnah, Ijma‘, Qiyas) and secondary (Istihsan, Istislah, ‘Urf), each with its own authority.

This lesson introduces the structure of the sources of Islamic jurisprudence (uṣūl al-fiqh) as presented in the book's outline. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti, as it determines which source to consult first when deriving a ruling. The primary sources form the foundation, while secondary sources provide flexibility within the framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary sources
From chapter three, list the four primary sources: the Qur’an, the Sunnah, Consensus (Ijma‘), and Analogy (Qiyas). Note that the Hanafi school considers Qiyas a primary source, unlike some other schools.
Primary sources are always consulted before secondary ones.
2
Identify the secondary sources
From chapters four through six, list the secondary sources: Juristic Preference (Istihsan), Consideration of Public Interest (Istislah), and Custom (‘Urf). These are used to supplement the primary sources.
Istihsan is particularly important in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the historical and relative order
Chapter seven discusses the chronological and hierarchical order of sources. This order guides the mufti on which source to prioritize when multiple sources are applicable.
The historical order may differ from the relative weight.
4
Distinguish Shari'ah and Fiqh
Chapter eight explains the difference between Shari'ah (divine law as revealed) and Fiqh (human understanding derived from the sources). This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of ijtihad.
Fiqh is the product of applying sources, not the sources themselves.

Primary sources: The second part: Sources of Islamic jurisprudence. Outline of the second part: Chapter Three: The Book, the Sunnah, Consensus, and Analogy. Commentary
This is the table of contents for the second part of the book, which discusses the sources (maṣādir) of Islamic jurisprudence. It lists primary sources (al-Kitāb, al-Sunnah, Ijmā‘, Qiyās) in Chapter Three, followed by subsidiary sources: Istihsān (Chapter Four), Istislāḥ (Chapter Five), ‘Urf (Chapter Six), the historical and relative order of sources (Chapter Seven), and the distinction between Sharī‘ah and Fiqh (Chapter Eight). Students should familiarize themselves with this structure as it outlines the major headings of uṣūl al-fiqh.

Key Terms
المصادر الأساسية (al-maṣādir al-asāsiyyah): The primary sources of fiqh: Qur’an, Sunnah, Ijma‘, and Qiyas.
المصادر التبعية (al-maṣādir al-taba‘iyyah): Subsidiary sources: Istihsan, Istislah, ‘Urf, etc.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of departing from strict analogy for a more suitable ruling.
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Consideration of public interest (maṣlaḥah mursalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies sources hierarchically: primary sources (Qur’an, Sunnah, Ijma‘, Qiyas) come first, followed by secondary sources (Istihsan, Istislah, ‘Urf).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Quran as the Primary Source of Fiqh
Introducing the four primary sources and focusing on the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the role of the Quran as the primary source in Islamic legislation and explain its constitutional nature.

Core Idea
The Quran is the constitution of Islamic law, providing general principles and detailed creed, while leaving particulars to other sources.

Islamic fiqh derives its rulings from four primary sources: the Quran, the Sunnah, consensus (ijma'), and analogical reasoning (qiyas). Among these, the Quran stands as the original and foundational source, much like a constitution in modern legal systems. This lesson examines its unique role and scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four primary sources
State the four masadir al-fiqh as listed in the text: al-Kitab, al-Sunnah, al-Ijma', and al-Qiyas.
2
Understand the Quran's constitutional role
Recognize that the Quran is the 'origin' (asl) in legislation, analogous to a constitution. It provides the foundational principles for beliefs (detailed) and for worship and rights (general).
The text says 'فقد بينت فيه أسس الشريعة' – its foundations are explained.
3
Distinguish between detailed and general rulings
Note that the Quran details creedal matters but gives general principles for fiqh rulings, leaving specifics to the Sunnah and ijtihad. The text explicitly states it does not address particulars and modalities except rarely.
This is a key distinction: the Quran is not a comprehensive legal code.
4
Apply the concept in usul al-fiqh
Understand that a mufti must prioritize the Quran as the highest authority, but cannot derive detailed rulings from it alone without the Sunnah and other sources.
This sets the stage for the hierarchy of sources in Hanafi usul.

Source Text

المصادر الأساسية للفقه

۷۳
Chapter Three: The Primary Sources of Fiqh: The Book, the Sunnah, Consensus, and Analogy.
1/3 - We saw in the previous second chapter (1/2) that the rulings of issues in Islamic fiqh are based either on explicit text in the Quran or the Prophetic Sunnah, or on consensus of scholars, or on the deduction of mujtahids from textual evidence and Sharia principles.
Here the scholars counted the sources of fiqh – i.e., its origins from which it draws – as four: the Book, the Sunnah, Consensus, and Analogy.
First Section: The Book.
2/3 - As for the Book, i.e., the Quran, it is the origin in Islamic legislation. In it the foundations of Sharia are explained and its features are clarified: in beliefs in detail, and in acts of worship and rights in summary.
It is in Islamic Sharia like the constitution in positive laws of nations. It is the model for the Prophet himself, peace be upon him, and those after him. Therefore it is the original legislative source.
However, the Book, by virtue of its constitutional nature, deals with the explanation of rulings through general text and does not address particulars and details of modalities except rarely, because such detail would make it lengthy and divert it from its other Quranic purposes of eloquence and others.
73
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage introduces the primary sources of Islamic fiqh and begins with the Quran. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that Islamic fiqh rulings derive from four foundational sources: the Quran, the Sunnah, consensus (ijma'), and analogical reasoning (qiyas). The Quran is the original source, analogous to a constitution in positive law. It provides detailed guidance for beliefs and general principles for acts of worship and rights. The Quran does not generally delve into particulars and modalities, as that would detract from its other purposes. This sets the stage for understanding the hierarchy and role of other sources.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-Kitab): The Quran as the primary source of Islamic legislation
مصادر الفقه (masadir al-fiqh): The four primary sources from which fiqh rulings are derived: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
