A) It provides detailed rulings for all acts of worship and transactions.
B) It is the original source, like a constitution, giving general principles and detailed creed.
C) It is only a source for beliefs, not for legal rulings.
D) It is one of four equal sources without priority.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the primary source of Islamic legislation, providing foundational principles and detailed beliefs, while leaving operational details to other sources.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
The Qur'an's General Nature and the Sunnah's Detailing Role
Explains Qur'anic generality and Sunnah's detailing role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Qur'an is often general (mujmal) and the Sunnah provides the necessary detail, and distinguish between the two meanings of Sunnah (hadith and 'amal) for iftaa purposes.

Core Idea
The Sunnah is the key to understanding and applying the general Qur'an.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work on the sources of Islamic jurisprudence lays out a foundational principle: the Qur'an gives broad commands, and the Sunnah fills in the specifics. Understanding this relationship is essential for anyone engaging in iftaa, as it explains why we must turn to the Sunnah to implement Qur'anic rulings. We'll also explore the dual meaning of 'Sunnah'—as transmitted reports and as the living practice of the early community—and how this distinction affects legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qur'an's general nature
Look at the examples given: prayer, zakat, contracts, shura, justice. The Qur'an commands these in general terms without detailing modalities or quantities.
This generality is intentional to allow flexibility.
2
Recognize the Sunnah's detailing role
The Sunnah provides the how (kayfiyya) and how much (qadr), as well as the criteria for valid contracts, etc.
The verse 59:7 is cited as the Qur'anic mandate for this.
3
Understand the two meanings of Sunnah
First meaning: Sunnah as hadith (sayings, actions, approvals). Second meaning: Sunnah as the practical application ('amal) during the prophetic era and beyond.
These two may conflict; the practice can override a solitary hadith in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply the distinction in iftaa
When giving a fatwa, a mufti must consider not only the transmitted hadith but also the established practice of the early community, especially when the practice contradicts a weak or solitary report.
This is a key Hanafi principle: 'amal al-madinah or 'amal al-salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: مصادر الفقه الإسلامي

فقد ورد فيه الأمر مثلاً بالصلاة والزكاة مجملاً، ولم يفصل فيهما كيفية ولا مقادير بل فضلتهما السنة بقول الرسول عليه السلام وفعله . وكذلك أمر القرآن بالوفاء بالعقود، ونصّ على حل البيع وحرمة الربا إجمالاً، ولكن لم يبين ما هي العقود والعهود الصحيحة المحللة التي يجب الوفاء بها، وما الباطلة أو الفاسدة التي ليست محلاً للوفاء؟ فتكفلت السنة أيضاً ببيان أسس هذا التمييز .

على أنَّ القرآن قد تناول تفصيل جزئيات الأحكام في بعض المواضع أو المواضيع كما في المواريث وكيفية اللعان بين الزوجين، وبعض الحدود العقابية والنساء المحارم في النكاح.

ولهذا الإجمال في نصوص القرآن مَزيَّة هامة أخرى بالنسبة إلى أحكام المعاملات المدنية والنظم السياسية والاجتماعية فإنه يساعد على فهم تلك النصوص المجملة وتطبيقها بصور مختلفة يحتملها النص، فيكون باتساعه قابلاً لمجاراة المصالح الزمنية وتنزيل حكمه على مقتضياتها بما لا يخرج عن أسس الشريعة ومقاصدها .

وذلك كما ورد في القرآن من النص على الشورى السياسية دون تعيين شكل خاص لها فكانت شاملة لكل نظام حكومي يجتنب فيه الاستبداد، ويتحقق فيه تشاور واحترام صحيح لرأي أولي الرأي والعلم في الأمة، سواء أكان نظاماً جمهورياً، أو نظام خلافة دستورية، أو غير ذلك مما لا استثثار فيه لفرد أو لفئة، بحسب ما تمليه المصلحة العامة

وكذلك أوجب القرآن إقامة العدل وإحقاق الحق بين الناس بنصوص عامة كثيرة، ولم يحدد طريقة القضاء الذي هو الأداة لذلك. فمن حيث العدد يمكن أن تكون المحاكم محاكم فرد أو محاكم جماعة، ومن حيث الدرجة يمكن أن يكون القضاء على درجة واحدة أو على عدة درجات (ر: ف ١١/٥).

وعلى كل بهذا الإجمال في نصوص الكتاب كانت هذه النصوص محتاجة إلى البيان بالسنة النبوية ليمكن تطبيقها في الكيفيات والكميات ولتعرف حدودها في الشمول والاقتصار وتنزل عليها جزئيات الحوادث والأعمال .

لذلك جاء في القرآن إحالة عامة على السنة النبوية في هذه التفصيلات بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا آتَاكُمُ الرَّسُولُ فَخُذُوهُ وَمَا نَهَاكُمْ عَنْهُ فَانتَهُوا﴾ [٧/٥٩].

ومن ثم كانت السنة مفتاح الكتاب .

المبحث الثاني : السنة

٣/٣ - يطلق لفظ (السنة) على ما جاء منقولاً عن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم من قول أو فعل أو تقرير وهي بهذا المعنى مرادفة للفظ «الحديث».

وقد تُطلق على معنى الواقع العملي في تطبيقات الشريعة في عصر النبوة، أي الحالة التي جَرَى عليها التعامل الإسلامي في ذلك العصر الأول.

والفرق بين المعنيين في النتيجة أنه قد ينقل عن الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام حديث لفظي، ولكن يثبت للعلماء تاريخياً أن عمل الناس في عصر النبوة أو في امتداد زمن الخلفاء الراشدين كان جارياً على خلاف مدلول ذلك الحديث (١).

(١) المراد من التقرير أن يصدر أمام النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل أو قول من إنسان، أو يُخبر به، فيعلمه الرسول ويقرّ الفاعل أو القائل أو الناقل على ذلك. ولا ينهاه ولا ينكر عليه، ولا يشير إلى أنه خلاف الأولى (ر: (الموقظة) للذهبي والتتمة الأولى في بيان السنة التقريرية ص ٩٨ - ١٠٢ للشيخ عبد الفتاح أبو غدة).
(٢) وعلى هذا المعنى يقع مثل قول عبد الرحمن بن مهدي وقد سُئِل عن سفيان الثوري والأوزاعي ومالك : سفيان إمام في الحديث وليس بإمام في السنة، والأوزاعي إمام السنة وليس بإمام في الحديث ومالك إمام فيهما جميعاً» (ر: شرح موطأ مالك للزرقاني، ومقدمة تنوير الحوالك شرح الموطأ أيضاً للسيوطي، وكتاب «نظرة عامة في تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي للأستاذ علي حسن عبد القادر ص ١١٦).
Chapter Two: Sources of Islamic Jurisprudence

For example, it commanded prayer and zakat in general terms, not detailing their manner or amounts; rather, the Sunnah detailed them through the saying and action of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Similarly, the Qur'an commanded fulfillment of contracts, and stated the permissibility of sale and the prohibition of usury in general, but did not clarify what are the valid and lawful contracts that must be fulfilled, and what are the invalid or defective that are not subject to fulfillment. So the Sunnah also undertook to explain the bases of this distinction.

However, the Qur'an did address the detail of some rulings in certain places or topics, such as inheritance, the procedure of mutual imprecation (li'an) between spouses, some punitive limits, and prohibited women in marriage.

And this generality in the texts of the Qur'an has another important advantage regarding the rulings of civil transactions and political and social systems: it helps to understand those general texts and apply them in various ways that the text can bear, so that, with its breadth, it is capable of keeping pace with temporal interests and applying its ruling according to their requirements without departing from the foundations and objectives of the Sharia.

For example, the Qur'an textually mentions political consultation (shura) without specifying a particular form for it, so it encompasses every governmental system that avoids despotism and in which consultation and proper respect for the opinion of the people of opinion and knowledge in the nation are realized, whether it is a republican system, a constitutional caliphate system, or other forms in which there is no exploitation by an individual or a group, according to what the public interest dictates.

Similarly, the Qur'an obligated the establishment of justice and the vindication of rights among people through many general texts, and did not specify the method of judiciary which is the tool for that. In terms of number, courts can be individual courts or collegiate courts; in terms of level, the judiciary can be single-level or multiple levels (see: f 11/5).

In any case, with this generality in the texts of the Book, these texts needed explanation by the Prophetic Sunnah to be applied in terms of modalities and quantities, to know their limits of inclusiveness and exclusivity, and to apply to them the particulars of events and actions.

Therefore, the Qur'an contains a general referral to the Prophetic Sunnah regarding these details by His saying, the Most High: "And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it" [59:7].

Hence, the Sunnah is the key to the Book.

Second Section: The Sunnah

3/3 - The term (Sunnah) is applied to what is transmitted from the Messenger, peace be upon him, of sayings, actions, or approvals, and in this sense it is synonymous with the term 'hadith'.

It may also be applied to the meaning of the practical reality in the applications of Sharia during the prophetic era, i.e., the state that prevailed in Islamic practice in that first period.

The difference between the two meanings in the result is that a verbal hadith may be transmitted from the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, but scholars historically establish that the practice of people in the prophetic era or extending into the time of the Rightly Guided Caliphs was contrary to the import of that hadith. (1)

(1) What is meant by approval (taqrīr) is that an action or saying of a person occurs before the Prophet, peace be upon him, or he is informed of it, and the Messenger knows it and approves the doer, speaker, or transmitter. He does not forbid him, nor does he disapprove, nor does he indicate that it is contrary to what is better (see: al-Mawqidha by al-Dhahabi and the first supplement explaining the Sunnah of approval pages 98-102 by Sheikh Abd al-Fattah Abu Ghudda).
(2) In this meaning falls the saying of Abd al-Rahman bin Mahdi when asked about Sufyan al-Thawri, al-Awza'i, and Malik: "Sufyan is an imam in hadith and not an imam in Sunnah, al-Awza'i is an imam in Sunnah and not an imam in hadith, and Malik is an imam in both" (see: Sharh Muwatta Malik by al-Zurqani, Introduction to Tanwir al-Hawalik sharh al-Muwatta also by al-Suyuti, and the book A General Overview of the History of Islamic Jurisprudence by Professor Ali Hasan Abd al-Qadir p. 116).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a general exposition on the relationship between the Qur'an and the Sunnah as primary sources of fiqh. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that the Qur'an often gives general commands (e.g., pray, pay zakat, fulfill contracts) without detailing the how or how much; the Sunnah provides that detail. This is a standard Hanafi usul principle: the Qur'an is the root (asl), the Sunnah is the expounder (mubayyin). He then highlights a key benefit of the Qur'an's generality: it allows flexibility for different times and circumstances, as long as the Sharia's foundations are respected, giving examples of shura (consultation) and justice (adl). The passage then transitions to define 'Sunnah' in two ways: (1) as a source of law consisting of the Prophet's words, actions, and approvals (taqrir) – synonymous with hadith; (2) as the actual practice ('amal) of the early Muslim community. This second meaning is crucial because a hadith may be transmitted verbally but the established practice of the Companions and Successors might follow a different understanding, and that practice can take precedence in Hanafi usul (the 'amal of Medina or the practice of the salaf). The footnote on taqrir clarifies that a prophetic approval is passive: the Prophet knew of an action and did not object. The second footnote quotes Abd al-Rahman bin Mahdi distinguishing between being an 'imam in hadith' (knowledge of narrations) and 'imam in Sunnah' (knowledge of the operative sunna/practice). This distinction is vital in Hanafi fiqh when dealing with conflicts between a reported hadith and established practice.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): ما جاء منقولاً عن الرسول من قول أو فعل أو تقرير، وهو مرادف للحديث (what is transmitted from the Prophet of sayings, actions, or approvals, synonymous with hadith)
التقرير (al-taqrir): أن يصدر أمام النبي فعل أو قول من إنسان، أو يُخبر به، فيعلمه الرسول ويقرّ الفاعل أو القائل أو الناقل على ذلك (an act or saying occurring before the Prophet, and he does not object, thus approving it)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one key advantage of the Qur'an's general (mujmal) style regarding civil transactions and political systems?
A) It makes the religion difficult to follow.
B) It allows flexibility to adapt to changing times while staying within Sharia principles.
C) It eliminates the need for the Sunnah.
D) It forces a single form of government.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an provides foundational principles; the Sunnah is the key that unlocks their practical application in detail.

Introduction to Al-Istislah and Al-Masalih Al-Mursalah
Chapter outline highlighting subsidiary sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the key components of the theory of al-masalih al-mursalah and distinguish it from istihsan.

Core Idea
Al-masalih al-mursalah justify rulings based on public interest, restricted by the three levels of necessity.

This lesson introduces the fifth chapter's outline on al-istislah and al-masalih al-mursalah, a subsidiary source of law. You will learn its definition, the classification of interests, and its relationship with other principles like istihsan and siyasah shar'iyyah. Understanding this outline will help you navigate the detailed discussion in the full chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of istislah
Istislah is the method of deriving rulings by considering public interest when no specific text applies. The outline defines it and clarifies the concept of maslahah and mafsadah.
2
Understand the three levels of maslahah
The outline divides maslahah into daruriyyat (necessities), hajiyyat (needs), and tahsiniyyat (improvements). Each level has different legal weight.
3
Note the factors leading to istislah
Two factors are mentioned: sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil) and taghayyur al-zaman (change of time). These justify considering public interest.
4
Compare istislah with istihsan
The outline explicitly points out the agreement and difference between istislah and istihsan. Istihsan is a preferred ruling based on a stronger evidence, while istislah is based on general interest without a specific textual basis.
5
Recognize the types of rulings and the conflict with texts
The outline lists rulings based on masalih mursalah in public administration, judiciary, and private rights. It also addresses conflict with definitive (qat'i) and non-definitive (ghayr qat'i) texts, with specific treatments in Maliki and Hanafi schools.

Chapter Five: Subsidiary Sources: Al-Istislah and Al-Masalih Al-Mursalah. Outline of the chapter: Definition of istislah; Clarification of the concept of interests and harms; A) The intrinsic meaning of interest and harm, B) The legal consideration of interest and harm: Necessities, Needs, and Improvements; Factors leading to istislah (blocking the means and change of time); The agreement and difference between istislah and istihsan; Types of rulings built on the theory of masalih mursalah: – In public administration – In the judicial system – In private rights; Istihsan and istislah in the scholarly schools; Conflict of masalih mursalah with definitive texts: Interest in the face of a definitive text; – Interest in the face of a non-definitive text: In the Maliki school; In the Hanafi school; Appendix to this chapter: Istislah and siyasah shar'iyyah.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is the table of contents or outline for the fifth chapter of the book, which deals with the subsidiary sources of Islamic jurisprudence: al-istislah (consideration of public interest) and al-masalih al-mursalah (unregulated interests). The outline shows that the chapter first defines istislah, then explains the concept of interests (masalih) and harms (mafasid), dividing them into three classical levels: necessities (daruriyyat), needs (hajiyyat), and improvements (tahsiniyyat). It then discusses factors that necessitate istislah, such as sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil) and change of time. It compares istislah with istihsan (juristic preference). It lists the types of rulings based on masalih mursalah in different fields: public administration, judiciary, and private rights. Then it discusses the use of istihsan and istislah in various schools of jurisprudence. Finally, it addresses the conflict between masalih mursalah and definitive texts (nusus qat'iyya) and non-definitive texts (nusus ghayr qat'iyya), specifically in the Maliki and Hanafi schools. An appendix covers istislah and siyasah shar'iyyah (sharia-oriented policy). This outline provides a roadmap for the chapter, highlighting the key topics and their organization.

Key Terms
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): The legal method of deriving rulings based on consideration of public interest (maslahah) in the absence of specific textual evidence.
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursalah): Unregulated interests that are neither explicitly validated nor invalidated by the primary sources, and are considered valid grounds for rulings if they serve the public good.
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): Blocking the means that could lead to harm or prohibited acts, a factor that necessitates istislah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the outline, what is the term for the three categories of legal consideration of interest and harm?
A) Mubah, Makruh, Haram
B) Daruriyyat, Hajiyyat, Tahsiniyyat
C) Fard, Wajib, Sunnah
D) Sahih, Batil, Makruh

Key Takeaway
Al-masalih al-mursalah is a subsidiary source of law based on public interest, structured by three levels of necessity and balanced against definitive and non-definitive texts.

From History to Application: The Modern Codification of Islamic Fiqh
Author concludes Part Three and outlines Part Four's chapters on codification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the significance of the fourth chapter's outline in al-Zarqa's argument for the modern codification (taqnin) of Islamic fiqh and identify two key examples of codification from the text.

Core Idea
Modern codification (taqnin) of Islamic fiqh is not just nostalgia — it is a legal necessity for constitutional states.

Al-Zarqa concludes his historical survey of Islamic jurisprudence by pointing to contemporary developments: openness between madhhabs in Islamic banking, and the movement to codify fiqh into modern civil codes. He then presents the outline of Part Four, which will detail these efforts. This lesson clarifies why the outline matters and what the author intends to prove.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main points in the conclusion of Part Three
Read the first paragraph after 'ب - وبرز...' and the section on 'التقنين من الفقه الإسلامي'. Note the two developments: (1) openness between madhhabs in Islamic banking fatwas, (2) the movement to harmonize laws with fiqh through codification.
2
Understand the significance of openness between madhhabs
This is not merely academic; it reflects a modern fatwa methodology where muftis draw from all schools. Consider how this impacts the practicality and acceptance of Islamic finance.
3
Recognize taqnin as a response to constitutional declarations
Al-Zarqa references Arab constitutions after independence that named fiqh as the main source of legislation. This created the need to codify fiqh into enforceable codes. Identify which countries are mentioned as pioneers: Jordan, UAE, Sudan.
4
Trace the structure of Part Four and see how each chapter supports the author's thesis
Examine the outline of chapters 18-22. Each chapter addresses a key aspect: the modern trend of utilizing all schools (ch.18), the value of ijtihad differences (ch.19), the religious character of fiqh (ch.20), contemporary problems in applying sharia (ch.21), and existing civil laws built from fiqh (ch.22). Notice the appendices on specific topics like zina and riba.
This structure shows al-Zarqa's comprehensive approach: from theory to practical application.

Chapter Three: Eras of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. B - And emerged in the juristic activity in this field the complete openness between the schools, as no Islamic bank's religious body restricts itself to a single school, but rather all are guided in their ijtihad and fatwas by all the schools. 8/17 - Codification from Islamic Jurisprudence: Since it was placed in the constitutions of Arab countries after their independence that Islamic jurisprudence is the main source of legislation - as previously indicated - demands began to harmonize existing laws with the rulings of Islamic jurisprudence and change what contradicts. The demands focused in Egypt, Syria, Iraq, and Jordan on producing a modern civil code derived from Islamic jurisprudence in its broad sense, as previously explained. And this idea was realized in some Arab countries, namely Jordan which was a pioneer, then the United Arab Emirates and Sudan. Also, the preparation of a unified Arab civil code project derived from Islamic jurisprudence began, and an important part of it has already been completed, and we will explain all of that in Chapter Twenty-two and its appendices about civil laws and the virtue of building them from Islamic jurisprudence. The Fourth Chapter: On Deriving Contemporary Codification from Islamic Jurisprudence. Chapter Nineteen: The Necessity of Difference in Ijtihad and Its Value. Chapter Twenty: The Advantage of the Religious Character in Islamic Jurisprudence. Chapter Twenty-One: Contemporary Problems Faced by the Application of Islamic Sharia Today. First Appendix to this chapter: On the Punishment for the Crime of Adultery. Second Appendix to this chapter: The Problem of the Usury-Based Interest System. Chapter Twenty-Two: Civil Laws in Islamic Countries and the Virtue of Building Them from Islamic Jurisprudence. Commentary
This passage is a structural transition in al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm. It first completes the discussion of the third chapter on the development and characteristics of Islamic fiqh, highlighting two points: (1) the openness between madhhabs in modern Islamic banking, where no single school is exclusively followed, and (2) the movement for codification (taqnin) from Islamic fiqh, noting that Arab constitutions after independence declared fiqh as the main source of legislation. The author cites successful examples in Jordan, UAE, and Sudan, and mentions a project for a unified Arab civil code. Then, the passage introduces the outline of the fourth chapter, which focuses on contemporary codification from Islamic fiqh. The outline lists five chapters (18–22) with appendices covering topics such as modern trends in benefiting from all schools, the value of ijtihad differences, the religious nature of fiqh, contemporary problems in applying sharia (including punishment for zina and the riba system), and civil laws in Islamic countries. This section serves as a roadmap for the rest of the book, indicating that al-Zarqa aims to demonstrate the practical applicability of classical fiqh in modern legislation.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh into modern legal codes, especially in civil law.
الفقه الإسلامي (al-fiqh al-islami): Islamic jurisprudence in its broad sense, encompassing all madhhabs and sources.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Fundamental Jurisprudential Theories vs Universal Maxims
Prefatory explanation of the second section on fundamental jurisprudential theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fundamental jurisprudential theories, distinguish them from universal legal maxims, and explain their role in deriving fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
Fundamental theories form the skeleton of fiqh; universal maxims are subordinate rules within those theories.

This lesson introduces the second section of al-Zarqa's work, where he transitions from the historical overview to the structural theories underlying Islamic jurisprudence. You will learn what 'fundamental jurisprudential theories' are, how they differ from the well-known legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyyah), and why studying them gives the student a deeper jurisprudential faculty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of fiqh structure
Recognize that the author presents two distinct levels: fundamental theories (nazariyyat asasiyyah) which are the broad constitutional concepts, and universal maxims (qawa'id kulliyyah) which are subordinate controls that operate within those theories.
The Majalla came up with 99 universal maxims, but these are not the theories themselves.
2
Define fundamental theories from the passage
Extract the author's definition: they are major concepts that form objective legal systems diffused throughout fiqh, governing all related branches of rulings. Examples include ownership, contract, legal capacity, representation, nullity, liability, and custom.
Each theory is like a nervous system in the body—permeating and governing its domain.
3
Distinguish theories from maxims explicitly
Note the author's explicit contrast: universal maxims are only principles used within the boundaries of the larger theories. For example, the rule 'al-ibrah fi al-uqud li al-maqasid' (consideration in contracts is for purposes) is only a control within the theory of contract.
Maxims help derive rulings for specific incidents, but they presuppose the existence of the broader theory.
4
Understand the purpose of studying theories
The author aims to extract and abstract these theories from the scattered fiqh chapters to present them as complete systems. This reveals their governing authority and serves as a step toward a comprehensive theory of civil rulings in Islamic fiqh.
This is analogous to the Western 'general theory of obligations' but grounded in the Shari'ah.
5
Recognize the pedagogical benefit
Studying these theories gives the student an immediate fiqh aptitude (malakah fiqhiyyah) that would otherwise take a long time to acquire through case-by-case study alone.
The author mentions this in the preface as a key advantage of this approach.

تمهيد لهذا القسم الثاني من الكتاب
انتهينا من تلك المقدمة التعريفية التاريخية التي عرضنا فيها صورة كافية عن الفقه الإسلامي ومصادره وأطواره وأدواره التي مر بها منذ نشأته إلى اليوم في ذلك القسم الأول من هذا المدخل الفقهي العام. وننتقل الآن إلى القسم الثاني الذي سنعرض فيه مختلف النظريات الفقهية الأساسية في مباني الأحكام وهي النظريات التي يقوم عليها صرح الفقه الإسلامي في الواقع وتعتبر كالعنصر الهندسي في بنائه العظيم الخالد.
معنى هذه النظريات:
نريد من النظريات الفقهية الأساسية تلك الدساتير والمفاهيم الكبرى التي يؤلف كل منها على حدة نظاماً حقوقياً موضوعياً منبثاً في الفقه الإسلامي كانبثاث أقسام الجملة العصبية في نواحي الجسم الإنساني وتحكم عناصر ذلك النظام في كل ما يتصل بموضوعه من شعب الأحكام، وذلك كفكرة الملكية وأسبابها وفكرة العقد وقواعده ونتائجه، وفكرة الأهلية وأنواعها ومراحلها وعوارضها وفكرة النيابة وأقسامها، وفكرة البطلان والفساد والتوقف، وفكرة التعليق والتقييد والإضافة في التصرف القولي وفكرة الضمان وأسبابه وأنواعه وفكرة العرف وسلطانه على تحديد الالتزامات، إلى غير ذلك من النظريات الكبرى التي يقوم على أساسها الفقه بكامله ويصادف الإنسان أثر سلطانها في حلول جميع المسائل والحوادث الفقهية.
وهذه النظريات هي غير القواعد الكلية التي صدرت مجلة الأحكام الشرعية بتسع وتسعين قاعدة منها، والتي سنتولى شرحها في القسم الثالث الأخير. فإنَّ تلك القواعد إنما هي ضوابط وأصول فقهية تراعى في تخريج أحكام الحوادث ضمن حدود تلك النظريات الكبرى.
فقاعدة «العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني» مثلاً ليست سوى ضابط في ناحية مخصوصة من ميدان أصل نظرية العقد. وهكذا سواها من القواعد.
وسنجتهد في استخلاص تلك النظريات الأساسية وتجريدها وجمع شتات عناصرها من شتى الأبواب الفقهية المنبثة فيها، لتبرز كل منها في صورتها النظامية كاملة، وتتجلى حاكميتها في فروع الأحكام، وتكون تمهيداً لصياغة نظرية عامة جامعة لقواعد الأحكام المدنية في الفقه الإسلامي كنظرية الالتزام العامة في الفقه القانوني الأجنبي.
وإن مطالعة هذه النظريات الأساسية بعد طلوعها من مكامنها وراء فروع الأحكام، تعطي الطالب مَلَكة فقهية عاجلة تؤهل فكره وتعينه على مدارك الفقه؛ وقد كان يحتاج في اكتسابها إلى زمن طويل كما سلفت الإشارة إليه في خطبة هذا الكتاب.
وسنفرد لكل واحدة من هذه النظريات الفقهية الأساسية باباً نعرضها ونعالجها فيه مبتدئين بنظرية الملكية وأسبابها.
Preface to this second section of the book: We have finished that historical introductory presentation in which we presented a sufficient picture of Islamic jurisprudence, its sources, its stages, and its eras through which it passed from its inception to the present day in that first section of this general jurisprudential introduction. Now we move to the second section in which we will present various fundamental jurisprudential theories concerning the foundations of rulings—theories upon which the edifice of Islamic jurisprudence actually stands and which are considered like the structural element in its great eternal building. The meaning of these theories: By fundamental jurisprudential theories we mean those constitutions and major concepts, each of which independently forms an objective legal system diffused throughout Islamic jurisprudence, like the diffusion of the parts of the nervous system in the regions of the human body, and the elements of that system govern everything related to its subject from the branches of rulings, such as the concept of ownership and its causes, the concept of contract and its rules and consequences, the concept of legal capacity and its types, stages, and impediments, the concept of representation and its divisions, the concept of nullity, corruption, and suspension, the concept of suspension, restriction, and annexation in verbal dispositions, the concept of liability and its causes and types, and the concept of custom and its authority in determining obligations, and other major theories upon which all jurisprudence is based and whose influence one encounters in solving all jurisprudential issues and incidents. These theories are other than the universal rules that the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya issued with ninety-nine of them, which we will explain in the third and final section. For those rules are only jurisprudential controls and principles observed in deriving rulings for incidents within the boundaries of those major theories. For example, the rule 'Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings' is only a control in a specific aspect of the domain of the origin of the theory of contract. And likewise other rules. We will strive to extract these fundamental theories, abstract them, and gather the scattered elements from various jurisprudential chapters in which they are diffused, so that each appears in its systematic form complete, and its authority is manifested in the branches of rulings, serving as a prelude to formulating a general comprehensive theory for the rules of civil rulings in Islamic jurisprudence, like the general theory of obligations in foreign legal jurisprudence. Moreover, studying these fundamental theories after they emerge from their hidden places behind the branches of rulings gives the student an immediate jurisprudential aptitude that qualifies his mind and helps him grasp jurisprudence; whereas he would have needed a long time to acquire it, as previously indicated in the preface of this book. We will devote a chapter to each of these fundamental jurisprudential theories, presenting and treating them, beginning with the theory of ownership and its causes.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is the preface to the second section of al-Zarqa's work. The author concludes the first section, which covered the historical introduction to Islamic jurisprudence, and transitions to the second section. He defines 'fundamental jurisprudential theories' as major constitutional concepts that form objective legal systems permeating all fiqh branches. These include theories of ownership, contract, legal capacity, representation, nullity, suspension, liability, and custom. He explicitly distinguishes these theories from the universal legal maxims (like those in the Majalla) which will be explained in the third section. The maxims are subordinate controls operating within the framework of the theories. The author emphasizes that extracting these theories will give the student a quick jurisprudential faculty, and he plans to cover each theory in a separate chapter, starting with the theory of ownership.

Key Terms
النظريات الفقهية الأساسية (al-nazariyyat al-fiqhiyyah al-asasiyyah): الدساتير والمفاهيم الكبرى التي يؤلف كل منها نظاماً حقوقياً موضوعياً منبثاً في الفقه الإسلامي، كفكرة الملكية والعقد والأهلية وغيرها، وتحكم عناصرها في شعب الأحكام.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah): ضوابط وأصول فقهية تراعى في تخريج أحكام الحوادث ضمن حدود النظريات الكبرى، مثل قاعدة «العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني».

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Universal maxims are the building blocks that form the fundamental theories.
C) Fundamental theories are the larger frameworks; universal maxims are specific controls that operate within those frameworks.
D) There is no relationship; the two are independent.

Key Takeaway
Fundamental jurisprudential theories are the broad constitutional frameworks of fiqh, while universal maxims are specific rules that operate within these theories—understanding that hierarchy is essential for sound legal analysis.

The Method of Extracting Fundamental Theories in Islamic Jurisprudence
Author introduces methodology for extracting fundamental theories and outlines the ownership theory chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fundamental juristic theories (al-naẓariyyāt al-asāsiyyah) and specific legal rules (al-qawā‘id), and explain the author's methodological goal of systematizing derivations.

Core Idea
Specific rules are controls within broader theories; extracting theories from branches builds juristic mastery.

In this introductory passage, the author lays out his plan for the second section of the work. He explains that the specific legal rules familiar to students are not the ultimate end of study; rather, they serve as instruments within larger, more fundamental theories. By extracting those theories from the scattered branches of fiqh and presenting them systematically, the author aims to equip the student with a comprehensive framework that accelerates the development of juristic skill (malakah fiqhiyyah). This lesson focuses on understanding that methodological shift and its application to the upcoming theory of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key conceptual layers
Locate the author's mention of 'النظريات الأساسية' (basic theories) and 'القواعد' (rules). Note that he calls the rules 'ضوابط وأصول فقهية' (controls and juristic principles) that operate within the limits of the larger theories.
The rule 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني' is given as an example of such a control.
2
Understand the extraction process
The author says he will 'استخلاص تلك النظريات الأساسية وتجريدها وجمع شتات عناصرها من شتى الأبواب الفقهية' — extract, abstract, and gather scattered elements from various fiqh chapters. This is not a mere compilation but a systematic reconstruction.
The goal is to present each theory in its 'صورة نظامية كاملة' (complete systematic form).
3
Grasp the educational benefit
The author claims that studying these extracted theories gives the student an immediate 'ملكة فقهية عاجلة' (juristic skill) that would otherwise take a long time to develop. This relates to the overall objective of the book.
4
Preview the theory of ownership outline
Review the outline of the fifth chapter: definition & causes, division, characteristics, and distinctions (tamlik vs. ibaha, ownership of usufruct vs. right of usufruct). This is the first theory to be treated.
The following lessons will detail each of these topics.

مخطط الباب الخامس
١ - تعريف الملك، وبيان أسبابه ونتناوله في الفصل ٢٣.
٢ - تقسيم الملك وأسبابه: وتتناوله في الفصل ٢٤.

Preamble to the Second Section
Shari'ah has ninety-nine rules among these, which we will explain in the third and final section. For those rules are only controls and juristic principles that are considered in deriving the rulings of incidents within the limits of those major theories.
For example, the rule 'Consideration in contracts is for objectives and meanings' is only a control in a specific aspect of the field of the theory of contract. And likewise other rules.
And we will endeavor to extract those basic theories, abstract them, and collect their scattered elements from the various juristic chapters in which they are dispersed, so that each appears in its complete systematic form, and their governance in the branches of rulings becomes evident, and it becomes a preamble for formulating a general comprehensive theory for the rules of civil transactions in Islamic jurisprudence, like the general theory of obligations in foreign legal jurisprudence.
And indeed, studying these basic theories after they emerge from their hidden places behind the branches of rulings gives the student an immediate juristic skill that qualifies his thought and helps him in the understandings of jurisprudence; whereas he would have needed a long time to acquire it, as was previously indicated in the preface of this book.
And we will dedicate a chapter to each of these basic juristic theories, presenting and treating them, beginning with the theory of ownership and its causes.

Outline of the Fifth Chapter
1. Definition of ownership, and explanation of its causes and consequences: we will cover it in Chapter 23.
2. Division of ownership and its causes: we will cover it in Chapter 24.
3. Characteristics of ownership: this is the subject of Chapter 25.
4. The difference between tamlik (making something owned) and ibaha (permission), and the difference between ownership of usufruct and right of usufruct: these are the subjects of Chapter 26 with which we conclude the theory of ownership.
And we will explain below the theory of ownership in these branches and what branches out from them, and we will explain the basic juristic principles therein, and we will clarify their practical results that appear in the subdivisions of rulings.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to the second major section of his book, where he explains his methodology for extracting and presenting the fundamental legal theories underlying the rulings of Islamic jurisprudence. He contrasts these major theories (النظريات الأساسية) with the specific legal rules (القواعد) that operate within them, using the famous contractual maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني' as an example of a rule that is merely a control within the broader theory of contract. The author aims to gather scattered elements from various fiqh chapters to present each theory in a coherent, systematic form, analogous to the general theory of obligations in Western law. He then outlines the fifth chapter on the theory of ownership (نظرية الملكية), listing its four main topics: definition and causes, division, characteristics, and distinctions between related concepts. This front matter establishes the pedagogical and methodological framework for the subsequent chapters, emphasizing that studying these theories will quickly develop a student's juristic acumen.

Key Terms
النظريات الأساسية (al-naẓariyyāt al-asāsiyyah): The fundamental juristic theories that the author intends to extract and present systematically, such as the theory of ownership.
القواعد (al-qawā‘id): Specific legal rules that are controls (ḍawābiṭ) within the framework of larger theories; e.g., 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني'.
نظرية الملكية (naẓariyya al-milkiyya): The theory of ownership, the subject of the fifth chapter, covering definition, causes, division, characteristics, and distinctions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The rule replaces the need for a theory of contract.
B) The rule is a control that operates within the theory of contract.
C) The rule is a separate theory of its own.
D) The rule contradicts the theory of contract.

Key Takeaway
Fundamental theories organize scattered rulings into coherent systems, and specific rules are their subordinate controls.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Rule on Separation of Ownership and Usufruct in Bequests
Parsing a legal rule on ownership separation with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule regarding temporary separation of ownership and usufruct in bequests and distinguish valid temporary bequests from perpetual grants that become waqf.

Core Idea
Permanent separation of ownership and usufruct is futile; only temporary separation in bequests is valid.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence lays out a key principle of property law: ownership of the corpus (رقبة) and the right to use it (منفعة) cannot be permanently separated. Islamic law permits such a split only as a temporary exception in bequests, after which the benefits revert to the owner. The passage also clarifies that a perpetual bequest of benefits actually constitutes a waqf (endowment), not a bequest. We will parse this rule, its exceptions, and its theoretical background.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage states that permanent separation of ownership and usufruct is futile (عبث) and not approved by law. Only temporary separation is allowed as an exception.
The rule is based on the natural purpose of ownership: to derive benefit from the owned object.
2
List the Two Exceptional Cases of Valid Temporary Separation
(1) Bequest of usufruct to a person for a specified period or for life. (2) Bequest of corpus to one person and usufruct to another for a specified period.
In both cases, the separation is temporary; after the period, usufruct returns to the corpus owner.
3
Understand the Return Mechanism
After the specified period, the usufruct returns to the owner of the corpus – either the heirs (in the first case) or the legatee of the corpus (in the second). This ensures the separation is only temporary.
If the corpus owner is deceased, the usufruct passes to their heirs.
4
Distinguish Bequest from Endowment (Waqf)
If benefits are bequeathed forever to a charitable cause (e.g., to the poor perpetually), the grant is not a bequest but a waqf appended to death. The corpus becomes dedicated perpetually, and private ownership is extinguished.
The passage notes that there is no meaning to heirs' ownership if the benefit is permanently alienated.
5
Note the Theoretical Madhhab Difference
Some Maliki jurists argue that ownership only applies to benefits, not substance, because disposing of substance is beyond human power. The Hanafi (majority) view holds that ownership applies to both. Al-Zarqa notes no practical consequence on rulings.
This theoretical difference does not affect the rules of bequest of usufruct.

Chapter Five: Theory of Ownership in Jurisprudence. And legitimate disposal even by consumption (1). So if we imagine the separation of ownership of the corpus in a thing from the right of usufruct in a permanent manner, that is something futile which the law is not meant to approve. Rather, it approves it by way of exception in temporary incidental situations that end with the benefits being attached to the object and returning to the ownership of the corpus its reasonable fruit (2). On this basis, there is no cause of ownership in Islamic jurisprudence that requires owning the corpus alone without the usufruct except in the two cases of bequest of usufruct mentioned earlier (4/24), and they are: - If the owner bequeaths the usufruct of something from his property, such as the dwelling of his house or his garden to a person for a specified period or for his lifetime. - And if he bequeaths the corpus of the property to one person and its usufruct to another for a specified period. In the first case, the heirs, and in the second case, the legatee of the corpus, own only the corpus without the usufruct until the right of the legatee of the usufruct expires, then the right of usufruct returns to the owner of the corpus, whether heir or legatee of the corpus. If they are not alive, then to their heirs as previously mentioned in the division of ownership. The juristic view on the validity of this bequest is that the separation of the ownership of the corpus (1) *: Some Maliki jurists go in the philosophy of ownership to a view in which they see that ownership cannot fall upon the essences of things and the substances of their matter because disposing of their essence is not within human power, but rather falls on the benefits of things in varying degrees of scope and narrowness, absoluteness and restriction, timing and perpetuity. According to these limits, contracts differ in their names and results: sale gives ownership of benefits absolute and perpetual, leasing gives it restricted and temporary; and so on. But the majority of jurists, including the Hanafis, are contrary to this theory, and they see that ownership falls upon the essences and entities of things as it falls upon their benefits. And we see no practical consequence of this theoretical disagreement (ref: Al-Milkiyya wa Nazariyyat al-Aqd by Prof. Abu Zahra, p. 17). (2) See what was previously referred to regarding the state of amiri lands in our law (4/24, footnote). 360. Chapter (25) Characteristics of Ownership. The usufruct is temporary, not permanent, and the ownership of the heirs or the legatee of the corpus will end in full ownership in the eventual outcome, so this separation is excused in the present out of respect for the bequest because it is based on tolerance and contrary to analogy in many of its rulings, encouraging good deeds at the end of life. For this reason, a bequest of benefits alone forever to permanent entities, not to a specific person, departs from the meaning of a bequest and is considered in the sense of a permanent endowment (waqf) in which private ownership over the corpus of the property is dropped and it is permanently allocated to the benefit of the permanent charitable entity for which the endower designated it. So if a person bequeaths the fruit of his vineyard or the rent of his house to the poor forever, or to a specific person and after him to the poor, that is an endowment appended to after death out of necessity with a view to perpetuity even if expressed as a bequest, since there is no meaning left for the ownership of the heirs over the corpus as long as the benefit is for others forever. Ref: Fath al-Qadir, beginning of Waqf, 5/419, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/359.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule from al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. It establishes the principle that in Islamic law, ownership of the corpus (رقبة) and the right of usufruct (منفعة) cannot be permanently separated; such separation would be futile (عبث) because the very purpose of owning an asset is to derive benefit. However, temporary separation is exceptionally permitted in two specific cases of bequest (وصية): (1) bequeathing the usufruct of an asset (e.g., the right to live in a house) for a limited time or for the beneficiary's lifetime, and (2) bequeathing the corpus to one person and the usufruct to another for a limited period. In both cases, the separation is temporary and eventually the usufruct returns to the owner of the corpus or their heirs. The author also addresses a nuanced case: if someone bequeaths the benefits to a perpetual charitable cause (e.g., to the poor forever), this is not truly a bequest but rather an endowment (waqf) appended to death, because perpetual benefit cannot be separated from corpus ownership permanently—the corpus becomes dedicated to the charity. The footnote discusses a Maliki minority view (some Maliki jurists) that ownership only applies to benefits, not to the substance of objects, but the Hanafi position (and the majority) holds that ownership applies to both the substance and the benefits. The author notes this theoretical difference has no practical consequence. The passage is a clear exposition of the general rule and its exceptions, essential for understanding Islamic property law.

Key Terms
ملكية الرقبة (milkiyyat al-raqaba): Ownership of the corpus of a thing, i.e., the substance or capital asset.
منفعة (manfa'a): Usufruct or the right to use and benefit from a thing without consuming its substance.
وصية بالمنفعة (wasiyya bil-manfa'a): A bequest that grants only the usufruct of an asset to the legatee, not the corpus.
وقف (waqf): An endowment where ownership of the corpus is permanently transferred to Allah and benefits are dedicated to charitable purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a valid bequest of usufruct from a waqf?
A) The duration: temporary vs. perpetual
B) The type of asset: movable vs. immovable
C) The number of beneficiaries
D) The presence of a specific person as legatee

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ownership and usufruct cannot be permanently separated; temporary separation is valid in bequests, but perpetual grants of benefits are treated as endowments (waqf).
